Fallacy of Equivocation in the Term 'Mover'
Author explains a syllogistic fallacy due to equivocation of the term 'mover'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify equivocation of the middle term in a categorical syllogism and explain why it invalidates the argument.

Core Idea
Equivocation in the middle term makes a syllogism invalid even if each premise is true.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq exposes a subtle fallacy where the term 'mover' is used ambiguously between an accident and a substance. Understanding this fallacy sharpens your ability to detect hidden errors in arguments, especially in theological discussions about attributes and essences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
The argument is: Major premise: 'Every mover is necessarily false (ceases) in the moment.' Minor premise: 'Zayd is a mover.' Naive conclusion: 'Zayd is necessarily false in the moment.' Identify the terms: mover (middle term), Zayd (subject), necessarily false (predicate).
The middle term 'mover' is the key to the fallacy.
2
Distinguish the two senses of 'mover'
The author states that 'mover' can mean either the accident of motion (the attribute) or the substance that has motion (Zayd). Determine which sense is used in each premise.
If the major premise uses 'mover' as an attribute, it does not apply to Zayd as a substance.
3
Check the universal predicate condition
The major premise says 'Every mover is necessarily false in the moment.' If 'mover' means the accident, then the predicate applies only to accidents, not to substances. The condition of 'المقول على الكل' requires that the predicate apply to every individual of that kind, but the kind is ambiguous.
The conclusion mistakenly transfers the predicate from accident to substance.
4
Conclude why the argument fails
Because of equivocation, the middle term does not have a single meaning, violating the rule that the middle term must be univocal for a valid syllogism. The conclusion does not follow.
This fallacy is a type of ignoratio elenchi or equivocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتحرك ضروري بطلانه في الحال ويظن أنه يلزم منه بطلان زيد في الحال وهو محال فإنه إن أريد بالمقدمة الكبرى زيد المتحرك من حيث هو متحرك لم تكن النتيجة فـ زيد) ضروري بطلانه في الحال بل فـ زيد) من حيث هو متحرك ضروري ) البطلان في الحال وليس يلزم من بطلان زيد) متحرك من حيث هو متحرك؛ الضرورة بطلان الحركة وأنها لا تبقي بطلان زيد من حيث هو زيد، وإن أريد بـ (زيد اتصافه بالحركة على المشروط في فصل المقول على الكل المتحرك ما يصح كانت كاذبة لا محالة.
The moving thing (mover) is necessarily false in the moment, and it is thought that from this follows the falsehood of Zayd in the moment, which is impossible. For if by the major premise is meant 'Zayd the mover insofar as he is moving', then the conclusion is not 'Zayd is necessarily false in the moment' but rather 'Zayd insofar as he is moving is necessarily false in the moment'. And from the falsehood of 'Zayd is moving insofar as he is moving', the necessity of the falsehood of motion and that it does not remain does not entail the falsehood of Zayd insofar as he is Zayd. And if by (Zayd) is meant his description with motion on the condition in the section of the universal predicate (the mover is what is correct?) then it would be false necessarily.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage identifies a fallacy in categorical syllogism caused by equivocation of the middle term 'mover'. The major premise states 'Every mover is necessarily false (ceases to exist) in the moment.' The minor premise says 'Zayd is a mover.' The naive conclusion 'Zayd is necessarily false in the moment' is thought to follow, but the author shows it does not. The reason is that the term 'mover' can be taken in two senses: (1) as a predicate describing an accidental attribute (the state of moving) inhering in a substance, or (2) as the subject itself that possesses that attribute (Zayd qua substance). If the major premise uses 'mover' in sense (1), the conclusion only applies to Zayd's attribute, not Zayd himself. If the minor uses it in sense (2), the major premise would be false because 'mover' as a universal predicate would not apply distributively. The fallacy is thus a case of ignoratio elenchi or equivocation. The author uses an Islamic example: motion is an accident that does not persist, while Zayd as a substance persists.

Key Terms
المتحرك
ضروري البطلان
المقدمة الكبرى
المقول على الكل

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because 'mover' in the major premise refers to the accident of motion, while in the minor it refers to Zayd's substance.
B) Because the major premise is false.
C) Because Zayd is not a mover in reality.
D) Because the syllogism is in the wrong figure.

Key Takeaway
Equivocation of the middle term renders a categorical syllogism invalid, even if each premise is individually true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Equivocation Fallacy of 'fi' in Syllogisms
Author explains fallacious inferences caused by ambiguous use of the preposition 'fi' (in) as part of the predicate or as relational connector.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ambiguous use of the connector 'fi' in a categorical syllogism and explain why it invalidates the conclusion.

Core Idea
A connector like 'fi' can be part of the predicate or a relation — mixing them causes a fallacy.

The preposition 'fi' (in) is often used in Arabic propositions to link subject and predicate. But when it appears in a syllogism, its logical role may differ between premises: sometimes it is part of the predicate, sometimes it indicates a relation. This ambiguity can lead to an invalid conclusion that appears valid. In this lesson, you will learn to detect this fallacy by examining the function of 'fi' in each premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the major premise, minor premise, and apparent conclusion in the example: 'Health is not in anything of the sick' (major), 'Sickness is in every human' (minor), and the supposed conclusion 'Health is not in anything of people'.
In logical notation, the major is 'No sick person has health' and the minor is 'Every human has sickness'.
2
Analyze the role of 'fi' in each premise
In the major, 'fi' is part of a relational phrase 'is not in' indicating absence of a relation between health and the sick. In the minor, 'fi' is not relational but is part of the predicate 'in every human' meaning 'possesses' or 'has'. Recognize that 'fi' functions differently.
The author says in the major it indicates 'relation between the two parts', while in the minor it is 'part of the predicate'.
3
Check for uniform usage
If 'fi' is taken as part of the predicate in both premises, the minor becomes 'Every human is sickness' which is false (humans are not sickness itself). If 'fi' is taken as relational in both, then the premises are true but the conclusion does not follow because opposite accidents can alternate in the same subject.
The author explains that even if both premises are true with relational 'fi', the conclusion about health not being in people does not follow because a person can be healthy at another time.
4
Identify the fallacy type
The error is equivocation: the same word 'fi' is used with two different logical meanings in the two premises, causing an invalid syllogism. This is a sophism (mughalata) related to connectors.
Fallacies of equivocation occur when a term shifts meaning without notice.

وأما ما يرجع إلى الروابط، فكما يقال: (إن الصحة ولا في شيء من المريض) و المرض في كل إنسان بتقديم المحمولات، وتأخير الموضوعات، ويظن أن اللازم هو أن الصحة) ولا في شيء من الناس وهو محال كاذب، وليس ذلك إلا لأن حرف (في) في الكبرى دال على الارتباط بين الجزأين؛ ولذلك يصح أن يقال (لا شيء مما هو مرض بصحة)، وفي الصغرى هي جزء من المحمول؛ ولذلك لا يقول: (إن) كل إنسان (مرض)، بل كل إنسان فيه (مرض) أو (هو ذو مرض، وعند ذاك فلا يلزم من كون المرض ممكناً أن يكون في الإنسان ومن كون المرض ليس بصحة)، أن تكون الصحة غير موجودة في الإنسان؛ إذ لا مانع من اتحاد الموضوع، وتعاقب الأعراض المتقابلة عليه، كما في السواد والبياض بالنسبة إلى الجرم، وإن أخذ حرف (في) على وجه مبتدأ، واستعماله في المقدمتين إما بأن يجعل جزاء من المحمول فيهما، أو دالاً على الارتباط فيهما، كانت النتيجة أيضاً كاذبة أما على التقدير الأول فلكذب الصغرى، إذ ليس الإنسان هو المرض، وأما على التقدير الثاني، فلأنه وإن كانت المقدمات صادقة فليس يلزم من وجود شيء في شيء، وسلب وجود آخر في الوجود، كما في السواد والبياض ونحوه، ولو جعل موضوع الكبرى لا المرض بل هو ما هو موضوع المرض، بأن قيل ولا شيء مما هو مريض أو فيه مرض أو ذو صحة أو فيه صحة كانت النتيجة أيضاً كاذبة؛ لكذب المقدمة صحيح الكبرى.
As for what relates to connectors, it is like saying: 'Health is not in anything of the sick' and 'Sickness is in every human' with the predicates advanced and subjects delayed. It is thought that the necessary conclusion is that 'Health is not in anything of people', which is impossible and false. This is only because the letter 'fi' in the major premise indicates a relation between the two parts; therefore it is correct to say 'Nothing that is sickness is health', while in the minor it is part of the predicate; therefore one does not say 'Every human is sickness' but rather 'Every human has sickness' or 'he is possessed of sickness'. Then it does not follow from the fact that sickness is possibly in a human and that sickness is not health, that health is non-existent in the human; for there is no obstacle to the unity of subject and the succession of opposite accidents upon it, as in blackness and whiteness in relation to a body. If the letter 'fi' is taken in a uniform manner and used in both premises either as part of the predicate in both or as indicating relation in both, the conclusion would also be false. In the first case because of the falsity of the minor (since human is not sickness), and in the second case because even if the premises are true, it does not follow from the existence of one thing in another and the negation of existence of another that the other is non-existent, as in blackness and whiteness and the like. And if the subject of the major is made not 'sickness' but rather that which is the subject of sickness, by saying 'Nothing that is sick or has sickness or has health or is healthy' the conclusion would also be false due to the falsity of the major premise.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage discusses a fallacy arising from the ambiguous use of the preposition 'fi' (in) in syllogistic premises. The example: Major: 'Health is not in anything of the sick' (i.e., no sick person has health), Minor: 'Sickness is in every human' (every human has sickness). The apparent conclusion is 'Health is not in anything of people', which is false because a human can be healthy at one time and sick at another. The fallacy occurs because in the major premise 'fi' indicates a relation (i.e., health is not related to the sick), while in the minor 'fi' is part of the predicate (i.e., 'is in' is not a relation but a predication of sickness of humans). The author explains that if 'fi' is used consistently as a relational connector in both premises, the conclusion does not follow because opposite accidents can succeed one another in the same subject (e.g., blackness and whiteness in a body). If 'fi' is used as part of the predicate in both, the minor 'Every human is sickness' is false. If the major's subject is rephrased to refer to the subject of sickness (i.e., 'No sick person is healthy'), the major becomes false. This illustrates the need to carefully distinguish between the roles of connectors.

حرف في
المحمول
الموضوع
الأعراض المتقابلة

C) Because health and sickness cannot coexist in the same subject.
D) Because the syllogism is invalid in the first figure.

Key Takeaway
The connector 'fi' can function either as a relational term or as part of the predicate; mixing these roles in a syllogism produces an equivocation fallacy.

Selling fish in the sea is a transaction with gharar. Therefore selling fish in the sea is invalid.'). Then alter the role of 'bi' in one premise to create an equivocation fallacy.

The Syllogism

Transforming Propositions with Connectors: Part-Whole vs Relation
Explains transforming categorical propositions with connectors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify in a categorical syllogism whether a connector (في, لام) indicates part-whole or relation, and determine the validity of the resulting conclusion.

Core Idea
Connectors like 'fi' and 'li' can hide logical structure; deciding part-whole vs relation is key to valid inference.

This passage teaches you how to transform propositions with ambiguous connectors into clear categorical form, and then evaluate syllogisms that mix these connectors. Understanding this is crucial for avoiding subtle fallacies in logical arguments, especially when analyzing theological or legal reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the connector in each premise
Look for 'fi' (في) or 'li' (لام) in the Arabic premises. Note that they can indicate either part-whole (inherence) or relation (predication).
The same word can have different logical functions; context determines the interpretation.
2
Classify each premise's connector type
Determine if the connector indicates part-whole (e.g., accident in substance) or relation (e.g., substance for body). For 'fi', part-whole means the accident inheres in the subject; for 'li', part-whole means the predicate is a genus or species of the subject.
The author uses 'juzʾiyya' for part-whole and 'irtibāṭ' for relation.
3
Note the combination of types in the syllogism
There are four possible combinations: both premises use part-whole, minor is relation and major is part-whole, minor is part-whole and major is relation, or minor is relation and major is part-whole (the text lists four examples). Recognize which combination you have.
The combination affects transitivity.
4
Test transitivity of the relation
Part-whole relations are transitive (if A is part of B and B is part of C, then A is part of C). Relations are not necessarily transitive. For a valid syllogism, the middle term must connect the extremes via a transitive chain.
In the given examples, only the first and fourth combinations (both part-whole or both relation? Actually check: the author shows valid conclusions for all four; the key is uniform interpretation of the connector across premises.
5
Evaluate conclusion validity
If the connector is interpreted uniformly as part-whole in both premises, the conclusion follows by transitivity. If one premise uses relation, the conclusion may still follow if the relation is transitive (like 'is a' relation). In the negative case, the truth of the conclusion depends on whether the connector indicates part-whole or relation.
The author concludes that with part-whole interpretation, the negative is true; with relation, false.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if you transfer the proposition to an expression instead of these similitudes if possible, as is said: 'The body is a substance' instead of our saying: 'Substance exists for a body'. And it may happen that such a word indicates in both premises – i.e., the minor and major – part-whole relation, or [indicates] relation in the minor and part-whole in the major, or the opposite. The first: as in our saying 'Quantity exists in a substance' and 'Straightness and curvature exist in quantity', so 'Straightness and curvature exist in substance'. The second: as in our saying 'Substance exists for a body' and 'Body exists for an animal', so 'Substance exists for animal'. The third: as in our saying 'Color exists for whiteness' and 'Whiteness exists in a body', so 'Color exists in a body'. The fourth: as in our saying 'Whiteness exists in a body' and 'Body exists for an animal', so 'Whiteness exists in animal'. Moreover, similar may occur in negatives: as is said 'Nothing of motion is in motion' and 'Nothing of motion is for motion'. For if you take the letter 'fi' or 'lam' as indicating relation, the negative would be false, since motion is indeed motion, so negating that motion is motion would be false. And if they are taken as indicating part-whole, the negative would be true, since it is not correct to say that motion exists in motion, so its negation would be true. Upon this, if you said 'Every transition from place to place is motion' and 'Nothing of motion is in motion or for motion'...

Commentary
This passage provides practical guidance for transforming categorical propositions to avoid the fallacies previously discussed. The author suggests replacing expressions with connectors like 'fi' or 'li' by simpler forms like 'kāna' (is). For example, 'Substance exists for a body' becomes 'The body is a substance'. Then the author lists four combinations of how a connector word (e.g., 'fi' or 'li') can function in the minor and major premises: (1) both indicate part-whole (e.g., 'Quantity is in substance' and 'Straightness and curvature are in quantity' → 'Straightness and curvature are in substance'); (2) minor indicates relation, major part-whole (e.g., 'Substance is for body' [relation] and 'Body is for animal' [part-whole? actually body is part of animal? The example is 'Substance exists for body' and 'Body exists for animal', so both are relations? The text says 'indicates relation in the minor and part-whole in the major' but the example 'al-jawhar mawjūd li-l-jism' and 'al-jism mawjūd li-l-ḥayawān' both use 'li' as relation. There might be confusion; we follow the text: the author says 'the second' as when the minor is relation and major is part-whole. But the example given seems to use 'li' in both; perhaps 'li' in 'al-jawhar mawjūd li-l-jism' is taken as part-whole? Actually the earlier example said 'al-jawhar mawjūd li-l-jism' means 'the body is a substance' – so it indicates predication, which is part-whole? The classification is delicate. The author moves to negatives: If 'fi' or 'li' indicates relation, then 'Nothing of motion is in motion' is false because motion is itself motion; if they indicate part-whole, it is true because motion does not inhere in itself. The lesson underscores the importance of clarifying the logical role of connectors.

The validity of a syllogism with connectors like 'fi' and 'li' depends on whether they indicate a part-whole relation (transitive) or mere relation (not necessarily transitive); always clarify the logical meaning of connectors.

Pitfall 1: Compound Wording Masquerading as Simple Definition
First pitfall: simple definition with compound wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a definition is presented as a simple term but is actually compound, and replace it with a proper simple term.

Core Idea
A definition must be a single term; compound phrasing obscures logical analysis.

The first pitfall occurs when a definition is intended as a simple term (مفرد) but its wording is compound, like describing 'man' as 'rational animal that takes bread, chews, and swallows'. This makes the definition unusable in a syllogism. The remedy is to replace it with the true simple definition, such as 'rational animal'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the passage. In the example, it is 'insan' (man).
2
Examine the wording of the definition
Check if the definition is expressed as a single word or as a phrase containing multiple attributes. The example 'rational animal taking bread...' is compound.
3
Extract the essential genus and differentia
From the compound wording, identify the minimal defining components: genus (hayawan) and differentia (natiq).
4
Formulate the simple definition
Replace the compound expression with the simple conjunction 'hayawan natiq' (rational animal).
This ensures the definition functions as a single term in the syllogism.

الخبز ويتركه في فيه ويمضغه ويبلعه)، فعليك بتبديله بالمفرد فإنه ربما يكون ذلك أسهل. ومنها ما يعود إلى اشتباه الألفاظ الدالة عليها بالأدوات مثل (في كذا ولكذا)، أو حقها أن تكون جزءاً من المحمول ومنها ما المعنى، فإنك لو قلت كذا إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان كان مفيداً لما أفاده الأول، وأما مثال الثاني فكما في لفظ (يُعلم) من قولنا: (وكل جوهر فإنه يعلم أنه جوهر) في المثال السابق، ولولا دخوله في الحد الأكبر، وإلا كانت النتيجة فـ (كل جسم جوهر) وهي عين المقدمة الصغرى، فلا يكون مفيداً. وأما ما يرجع إلى شكل المقدمة وصورتها، بأن تكون موجبة معدولة، فيظن أنها سالبة بسيطة، أو سالبة للجهة، فيظن أنها جهة سالبة، وكذا بالعكس فيهما.
The eighth art: concerning consideration of useful matters for the forms of arguments. As for what is due to definitions, it is in several ways. Among them: that the definition is simple, but it is not obtained as such due to composition in its wording, as when one says instead of our statement: 'Man is a rational animal taking bread, leaving it in his mouth, chewing it, and swallowing it' — so you must replace it with the simple term, for perhaps that is easier. Among them: what goes back to confusion of the particles indicating them, such as 'in so-and-so' and 'for so-and-so', or their proper function is to be part of the predicate, and among them what is the opposite. Example of the first: our statement 'Accident exists in substance', for its meaning is that substance has the accident in it, not that it is an accident. Example of the second: our statement 'Substance exists for body' means that body is a substance, not that substance exists in body. So you must also consider what is part and what is a connective. If you transfer the proposition to an expression instead of these ambiguities if possible, as one says 'Body is substance' instead of our statement 'Substance exists for body'. And it may happen that such a word indicates in both premises — namely the minor and major — part-whole relation, or connection in the minor and part-whole in the major, or the opposite. The first is as in our statement: 'Quantity exists in substance' and 'Straightness and curvature are in quantity', so 'Straightness and curvature are in substance'. The second: as in our statement: 'Substance exists for body' and 'Body exists for animal', so 'Substance exists for animal'. The third: as in our statement: 'Color exists for whiteness' and 'Whiteness exists in body', so 'Color exists in body'. The fourth: as in our statement: 'Whiteness is in body' and 'Body is for animal', so 'Whiteness is in animal'. Indeed, this is also possible in negatives, as one says: 'No motion exists in motion' and 'No motion exists for motion'. If you take the particle 'in' or 'for' as indicating connection, then the negation is false, for motion certainly applies to itself, so negating that motion is motion is false. If they are taken as indicating part-whole, then the negation is true, for it is not correct to say that motion exists in motion, so its negation is true. Thus if you say 'Every transfer from place to place is motion' and 'No motion is in or for motion' and you intend by 'in' or 'for' the part-whole relation, it yields a true conclusion: that the transfer to a place has no motion or has no motion in it. Otherwise, the conclusion is false. Among them: what goes back to neglecting to state a condition that is actually a part taken with the subject or predicate or external, as previously defined. Among them: what goes back to repetition of definitions, for a definition may be taken in the syllogism as a middle term and as part of the major term in the major premise and in the conclusion, causing illusion. That is like our statement: 'Body is substance' and 'Every substance is known to be substance', so 'Body is known to be substance'. Among them: what goes back to transferring the definition, such that it is part of the predicate but we think it is part of the subject, as when it is said: 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or not simple'. For if 'insofar as' were part of the subject, then substance would only be simple and would not be not simple, which is a contradiction. Rather, 'insofar as' whenever it is used in speech in a meaningful way, it is only part of the predicate. That is by prefixing the disjunctive particle on our statement: 'insofar as' and 'from the aspect that' on the two parts of the disjunction, along with prefixing the negation particle on one of the two sides combined with 'insofar as', for it is the counterpart of the affirmative of the two. That is like our statement: 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or it is not, insofar as it is substance, simple.' Among them: that something that is not a definition is inserted into the statement and thought to be a definition, or it is a definition and thought not to be. Example of the first: our statement 'Every animal is a genus for man, and likewise man' — the word 'genus' is inserted only to indicate that it is predicated of man, not that it is essential; hence if you delete it, the meaning does not change, for if you said 'and likewise man' and 'Every man is an animal', it would convey what the first conveyed. As for the example of the second: the word 'known' in our statement: 'Every substance is known to be substance' in the previous example. Were it not for its entry into the major term, the conclusion would be 'Every body is substance', which is exactly the minor premise, so it would be not useful. As for what concerns the form and shape of the proposition: that it is an affirmative with negation, so it is thought to be a simple negative, or negative with modality, so it is thought to be a modal negative, and similarly the reverse in both.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of seven types of errors that arise from the misuse of definitions in logical arguments (صور الحجج). The author, likely Abhari or a commentator, details each pitfall with illustrative examples. The first error occurs when a definition is presented as a single term (مفرد) but the wording is actually compound, like defining 'man' as 'rational animal that takes bread, chews, swallows' instead of simply 'rational animal'. The remedy is to replace it with the proper simple term. The second error relates to ambiguous particles such as 'في' (in) and 'لام' (for) which may indicate either a part-whole relation (جزئية) or a connection (ارتباط). For instance, 'Accident exists in substance' means the accident is in the substance, not that the accident is a part of substance; whereas 'Substance exists for body' means body is a substance. The author advises converting such expressions into clearer forms like 'Body is substance.' He then analyzes four cases where the same particle functions differently in the minor and major premises. A special sub-case concerns negative propositions: whether 'No motion is in motion' is true or false depends on whether 'in' is taken as connection (false) or part-whole (true). The third error is neglecting to state a condition that is implied in the subject or predicate. The fourth is repetition of a term as both middle and part of major term, leading to a trivial conclusion like 'Body is known to be substance' which is essentially the same as the premise. The fifth error is misplacing a term: taking what is part of the predicate as part of the subject, as in the example 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or not', where 'insofar as' must be part of the predicate. The sixth error is inserting a non-definition into the argument (e.g., 'genus' when not essential) or omitting an essential part (e.g., 'known' in the syllogism). The seventh concerns confusion of proposition form: treating an affirmative with negation (موجبة معدولة) as a simple negative, or a modal negative as a negative modality, and vice versa.

Key Terms
حد
مفرد
مركب

Key Takeaway
A definition must be a simple term; compound wording defeats its purpose and should be replaced.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Pitfall 2: Ambiguous Particles: Part-Whole vs. Connection
Second pitfall: confusion of particles and their logical function.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether particles like 'في' and 'لام' indicate part-whole or connection in a proposition, and adjust the syllogism accordingly.

Core Idea
Particles like 'in' and 'for' can change meaning; mistaking part-whole for connection invalidates reasoning.

The second pitfall deals with ambiguous particles (أدوات) such as 'في' (in) and 'لام' (for). These may indicate either a part-whole relation (جزئية) or a connection (ارتباط). For example, 'Accident exists in substance' means the accident inheres, not that it is a part. But 'Substance exists for body' means body is a substance. The author shows how this ambiguity affects syllogisms, including negative propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle in the proposition
Locate the particle (في or لام) in the statement. Note its position.
2
Determine the intended relation
Decide whether the particle indicates part-whole (the second term is a part of the first) or connection (the first inheres in the second). Use context.
Example: 'Accident in substance' → connection; 'Substance for body' → part-whole (body is a substance).
3
Check consistency across premises
In a syllogism, the same particle might switch meaning between the minor and major premises. The author lists four cases where the function differs.
Case 1: both part-whole; Case 2: connection in minor, part-whole in major; etc.
4
Test with a negative proposition
For negatives like 'No motion is in motion', the truth depends on interpretation. If 'in' means connection, the statement is false (motion is motion). If part-whole, it is true (motion is not a part of itself).
This demonstrates the practical impact of the ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثامن في اعتبار أمور نافعة الاعتبار في صور الحجج وأما ما يكون بسبب الحدود فعلى وجوه منها: أن يكون الحد مفرداً، ولا يتحصل كذلك لتركب في لفظه، كما يقال بدل قولنا: (الإنسان يأكل الحيوان الناطق بأخذ الخبز ويتركه في فيه ويمضغه ويبلعه)، فعليك بتبديله بالمفرد فإنه ربما يكون ذلك أسهل. ومنها ما يعود إلى اشتباه الألفاظ الدالة عليها بالأدوات مثل (في كذا ولكذا)، أو حقها أن تكون جزءاً من المحمول ومنها ما يكون بالعكس، مثال الأول قولنا: (العرض) موجود في الجوهر إذ معناه أن الجوهر فيه العرض، لا أنه عرض ومثال الثاني قولنا: (الجوهر موجود للجسم معناه أن الجسم جوهر لا أن الجوهر موجود في الجسم، فعليك أيضاً باعتبار ما هو جزء، وما هو رابطة. وإن نقلت القضية إلى عبارة بدل هذه الأشباه إن أمكن، كما يقال: (الجسم جوهر) بدل قولنا: (الجوهر موجود الجسم)، وربما اتفق أن كانت ما مثل هذه اللفظة دالة في المقدمتين - أعني الصغرى والكبرى - على الجزئية، أو على الارتباط في الصغرى، والجزئية في الكبرى، أو بالعكس، فالأول كما في قولنا: (الكم موجود في الجوهر) و(الاستقامة والانحناء في الكم) فـ (الاستقامة والانحناء في الجوهر)، وأما الثاني، فكما في قولنا: (الجوهر موجود للجسم) و (الجسم موجود للحيوان) فـ (الجوهر موجود للحيوان)، وأما الثالث، فكما في قولنا: (اللون موجود للبياض) و(البياض موجود في الجسم) فـ (اللون) موجود في الجسم، وأما الرابع، فكقولنا: (البياض في الجسم) و(الجسم للحيوان) فـ البياض في الحيوان، بل وربما أمكن مثل ذلك في السوالب كما يقال: (لا شيء من الحركة في الحركة) و(لا شيء من الحركة للحركة)، فإنك إن أخذت حرف في أو اللام دالين على الارتباط كان السلب كاذباً؛ إذ الحركة تصدق عليها أنها حركة لا محالة، فسلب كون الحركة حركة يكون كاذباً، وإن أخذا على أنهما دالان على الجزئية، كان السلب صادقاً؛ إذ ليس يصح القول بوجود الحركة في الحركة، فسلبه يكون صادقاً، فعلى هذا لو قلت (كل انتقال من مكان إلى مكان حركة) و(لا شيء من الحركة في الحركة أو للحركة) وأردت بـ (في) أو (اللام) الجزئية، أنتج نتيجة صادقة أنه ليس للانتقال إلى مكان حركة أو ليس فيه (حركة)، وإلا فالنتيجة تكون كاذبة. ومنها ما يعود إلى إهمال التصريح بشرط، هو في الحقيقة جزء مأخوذ مع الموضوع، أو المحمول، أو خارج، كما سبق تعريفه. ومنها ما يعود إلى تكرار الحدود، فإنه ربما كان الحد مأخوذاً في القياس على أنه حد أوسط، وعلى جزء من الحد الأكبر في المقدمة الكبرى وفي النتيجة فيوقع الوهم، وذلك كقولنا: (الجسم جوهر) وكل جوهر فإنه يُعلم أنه جوهر فـ (الجسم يعلم أنه جوهر). ومنها ما يعود إلى نقل الحد، بأن يكون جزءاً من المحمول، ونظن أنه جزء من الموضوع، كما يقال: (الجوهر من حيث هو جوهر إما بسيط وإما لا بسيط) فـ (من حيث هو لو كانت جزءاً من الموضوع لما كان الجوهر إلا بسيطاً ولما كان لا بسيطاً وهو خلف، بل من حيث هو مهما دلت في الكلام على وجه مفيد لا يكون إلا جزءاً من المحمول، وذلك بأن يقدم حرف (إما وإما على قولنا: (من حيث هو) ومن جهة ما هو على جزئي الانفصال، مع تقديم حرف السلب على أحد الطرفين مقترناً به من حيث هو، إذ هو المقابل للموجب منهما، وذلك كقولنا: (الجوهر إما من حيث هو جوهر بسيط وإما أنه لا من حيث هو جوهر بسيط). ومنها: أن يدرج في القول ما ليس بحد ويظن أنه حد، أو أنه حد، ويظن أنه ليس بحد مثال الأول قولنا كل الحيوان جنس للإنسان وكذا إنسان فإن لفظ الجنس إنما أدرج في القول لبيان أنه محمول على الإنسان لا أنه ضروري؛ ولهذا لو حذفته لم يتغير المعنى، فإنك لو قلت كذا إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان كان مفيداً لما أفاده الأول، وأما مثال الثاني فكما في لفظ (يُعلم) من قولنا: (وكل جوهر فإنه يعلم أنه جوهر) في المثال السابق، ولولا دخوله في الحد الأكبر، وإلا كانت النتيجة فـ (كل جسم جوهر) وهي عين المقدمة الصغرى، فلا يكون مفيداً. وأما ما يرجع إلى شكل المقدمة وصورتها، بأن تكون موجبة معدولة، فيظن أنها سالبة بسيطة، أو سالبة للجهة، فيظن أنها جهة سالبة، وكذا بالعكس فيهما.
The eighth art: concerning consideration of useful matters for the forms of arguments. As for what is due to definitions, it is in several ways. Among them: that the definition is simple, but it is not obtained as such due to composition in its wording, as when one says instead of our statement: 'Man is a rational animal taking bread, leaving it in his mouth, chewing it, and swallowing it' — so you must replace it with the simple term, for perhaps that is easier. Among them: what goes back to confusion of the particles indicating them, such as 'in so-and-so' and 'for so-and-so', or their proper function is to be part of the predicate, and among them what is the opposite. Example of the first: our statement 'Accident exists in substance', for its meaning is that substance has the accident in it, not that it is an accident. Example of the second: our statement 'Substance exists for body' means that body is a substance, not that substance exists in body. So you must also consider what is part and what is a connective. If you transfer the proposition to an expression instead of these ambiguities if possible, as one says 'Body is substance' instead of our statement 'Substance exists for body'. And it may happen that such a word indicates in both premises — namely the minor and major — part-whole relation, or connection in the minor and part-whole in the major, or the opposite. The first is as in our statement: 'Quantity exists in substance' and 'Straightness and curvature are in quantity', so 'Straightness and curvature are in substance'. The second: as in our statement: 'Substance exists for body' and 'Body exists for animal', so 'Substance exists for animal'. The third: as in our statement: 'Color exists for whiteness' and 'Whiteness exists in body', so 'Color exists in body'. The fourth: as in our statement: 'Whiteness is in body' and 'Body is for animal', so 'Whiteness is in animal'. Indeed, this is also possible in negatives, as one says: 'No motion exists in motion' and 'No motion exists for motion'. If you take the particle 'in' or 'for' as indicating connection, then the negation is false, for motion certainly applies to itself, so negating that motion is motion is false. If they are taken as indicating part-whole, then the negation is true, for it is not correct to say that motion exists in motion, so its negation is true. Thus if you say 'Every transfer from place to place is motion' and 'No motion is in or for motion' and you intend by 'in' or 'for' the part-whole relation, it yields a true conclusion: that the transfer to a place has no motion or has no motion in it. Otherwise, the conclusion is false. Among them: what goes back to neglecting to state a condition that is actually a part taken with the subject or predicate or external, as previously defined. Among them: what goes back to repetition of definitions, for a definition may be taken in the syllogism as a middle term and as part of the major term in the major premise and in the conclusion, causing illusion. That is like our statement: 'Body is substance' and 'Every substance is known to be substance', so 'Body is known to be substance'. Among them: what goes back to transferring the definition, such that it is part of the predicate but we think it is part of the subject, as when it is said: 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or not simple'. For if 'insofar as' were part of the subject, then substance would only be simple and would not be not simple, which is a contradiction. Rather, 'insofar as' whenever it is used in speech in a meaningful way, it is only part of the predicate. That is like our statement: 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or it is not, insofar as it is substance, simple.' Among them: that something that is not a definition is inserted into the statement and thought to be a definition, or it is a definition and thought not to be. Example of the first: our statement 'Every animal is a genus for man, and likewise man' — the word 'genus' is inserted only to indicate that it is predicated of man, not that it is essential; hence if you delete it, the meaning does not change, for if you said 'and likewise man' and 'Every man is an animal', it would convey what the first conveyed. As for the example of the second: the word 'known' in our statement: 'Every substance is known to be substance' in the previous example. Were it not for its entry into the major term, the conclusion would be 'Every body is substance', which is exactly the minor premise, so it would be not useful. As for what concerns the form and shape of the proposition: that it is an affirmative with negation, so it is thought to be a simple negative, or negative with modality, so it is thought to be a modal negative, and similarly the reverse in both.

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of seven types of errors that arise from the misuse of definitions in logical arguments (صور الحجج). The author, likely Abhari or a commentator, details each pitfall with illustrative examples. The first error occurs when a definition is presented as a single term (مفرد) but the wording is actually compound, like defining 'man' as 'rational animal that takes bread, chews, swallows' instead of simply 'rational animal'. The remedy is to replace it with the proper simple term. For instance, 'Accident exists in substance' means the accident is in the substance, not that the accident is a part of substance; whereas 'Substance exists for body' means body is a substance. The author advises converting such expressions into clearer forms like 'Body is substance.' He then analyzes four cases where the same particle functions differently in the minor and major premises. The third error is neglecting to state a condition that is implied in the subject or predicate. The fourth is repetition of a term as both middle and part of major term, leading to a trivial conclusion like 'Body is known to be substance' which is essentially the same as the premise. The fifth error is misplacing a term: taking what is part of the predicate as part of the subject, as in the example 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or not', where 'insofar as' must be part of the predicate. The sixth error is inserting a non-definition into the argument (e.g., 'genus' when not essential) or omitting an essential part (e.g., 'known' in the syllogism). The seventh concerns confusion of proposition form: treating an affirmative with negation (موجبة معدولة) as a simple negative, or a modal negative as a negative modality, and vice versa.

Pitfalls 3 and 4: Neglected Conditions and Repetition of Definitions
Third and fourth pitfalls: omitted conditions and repeated definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a condition is omitted from a definition and when a definition is repeated as middle and major term, causing triviality.

Core Idea
Definitions must include all conditions; repeating a definition in multiple roles leads to useless circularity.

The third pitfall is neglecting to state a condition that is implicitly part of the subject or predicate. The author refers to earlier definitions. The fourth pitfall is repetition: when the same term serves as both middle and part of the major term, the conclusion becomes trivial, as in 'Body is known to be substance' — essentially repeating the minor premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition in the syllogism
Look for the definiendum and its definition used as a term.
2
Check for omitted conditions
Ask whether the definition implicitly includes a condition that is not stated. For example, 'substance' might be defined with an implied condition 'that is not in a subject'.
3
Detect repetition of the definition
See if the same definition appears as both the middle term and as part of the major term in the major premise and conclusion.
4
Assess the conclusion
If the conclusion simply restates the minor premise with an added term, it is trivial. Example: 'Body is substance' and 'Substance is known to be substance' yields 'Body is known to be substance' — no new knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

القول بوجود الحركة في الحركة، فسلبه يكون صادقاً، فعلى هذا لو قلت (كل انتقال من مكان إلى مكان حركة) و(لا شيء من الحركة في الحركة أو للحركة) وأردت بـ (في) أو (اللام) الجزئية، أنتج نتيجة صادقة أنه ليس للانتقال إلى مكان حركة أو ليس فيه (حركة)، وإلا فالنتيجة تكون كاذبة. ومنها ما يعود إلى إهمال التصريح بشرط، هو في الحقيقة جزء مأخوذ مع الموضوع، أو المحمول، أو خارج، كما سبق تعريفه. ومنها ما يعود إلى تكرار الحدود، فإنه ربما كان الحد مأخوذاً في القياس على أنه حد أوسط، وعلى جزء من الحد الأكبر في المقدمة الكبرى وفي النتيجة فيوقع الوهم، وذلك كقولنا: (الجسم جوهر) وكل جوهر فإنه يُعلم أنه جوهر فـ (الجسم يعلم أنه جوهر). ومنها ما يعود إلى نقل الحد، بأن يكون جزءاً من المحمول، ونظن أنه جزء من الموضوع، كما يقال: (الجوهر من حيث هو جوهر إما بسيط وإما لا بسيط) فـ (من حيث هو لو كانت جزءاً من الموضوع لما كان الجوهر إلا بسيطاً ولما كان لا بسيطاً وهو خلف، بل من حيث هو مهما دلت في الكلام على وجه مفيد لا يكون إلا جزءاً من المحمول، وذلك بأن يقدم حرف (إما وإما على قولنا: (من حيث هو) ومن جهة ما هو على جزئي الانفصال، مع تقديم حرف السلب على أحد الطرفين مقترناً به من حيث هو، إذ هو المقابل للموجب منهما، وذلك كقولنا: (الجوهر إما من حيث هو جوهر بسيط وإما أنه لا من حيث هو جوهر بسيط). ومنها: أن يدرج في القول ما ليس بحد ويظن أنه حد، أو أنه حد، ويظن أنه ليس بحد مثال الأول قولنا كل الحيوان جنس للإنسان وكذا إنسان فإن لفظ الجنس إنما أدرج في القول لبيان أنه محمول على الإنسان لا أنه ضروري؛ ولهذا لو حذفته لم يتغير المعنى، فإنك لو قلت كذا إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان كان مفيداً لما أفاده الأول، وأما مثال الثاني فكما في لفظ (يُعلم) من قولنا: (وكل جوهر فإنه يعلم أنه جوهر) في المثال السابق، ولولا دخوله في الحد الأكبر، وإلا كانت النتيجة فـ (كل جسم جوهر) وهي عين المقدمة الصغرى، فلا يكون مفيداً. وأما ما يرجع إلى شكل المقدمة وصورتها، بأن تكون موجبة معدولة، فيظن أنها سالبة بسيطة، أو سالبة للجهة، فيظن أنها جهة سالبة، وكذا بالعكس فيهما.
The eighth art: concerning consideration of useful matters for the forms of arguments. As for what is due to definitions, it is in several ways. Among them: that the definition is simple, but it is not obtained as such due to composition in its wording, as when one says instead of our statement: 'Man is a rational animal taking bread, leaving it in his mouth, chewing it, and swallowing it' — so you must replace it with the simple term, for perhaps that is easier. Among them: what goes back to confusion of the particles indicating them, such as 'in so-and-so' and 'for so-and-so', or their proper function is to be part of the predicate, and among them what is the opposite. Example of the first: our statement 'Accident exists in substance', for its meaning is that substance has the accident in it, not that it is an accident. Example of the second: our statement 'Substance exists for body' means that body is a substance, not that substance exists in body. So you must also consider what is part and what is a connective. If you transfer the proposition to an expression instead of these ambiguities if possible, as one says 'Body is substance' instead of our statement 'Substance exists for body'. And it may happen that such a word indicates in both premises — namely the minor and major — part-whole relation, or connection in the minor and part-whole in the major, or the opposite. The first is as in our statement: 'Quantity exists in substance' and 'Straightness and curvature are in quantity', so 'Straightness and curvature are in substance'. The second: as in our statement: 'Substance exists for body' and 'Body exists for animal', so 'Substance exists for animal'. The third: as in our statement: 'Color exists for whiteness' and 'Whiteness exists in body', so 'Color exists in body'. The fourth: as in our statement: 'Whiteness is in body' and 'Body is for animal', so 'Whiteness is in animal'. Indeed, this is also possible in negatives, as one says: 'No motion exists in motion' and 'No motion exists for motion'. If you take the particle 'in' or 'for' as indicating connection, then the negation is false, for motion certainly applies to itself, so negating that motion is motion is false. If they are taken as indicating part-whole, then the negation is true, for it is not correct to say that motion exists in motion, so its negation is true. Thus if you say 'Every transfer from place to place is motion' and 'No motion is in or for motion' and you intend by 'in' or 'for' the part-whole relation, it yields a true conclusion: that the transfer to a place has no motion or has no motion in it. Otherwise, the conclusion is false. Among them: what goes back to neglecting to state a condition that is actually a part taken with the subject or predicate or external, as previously defined. Among them: what goes back to repetition of definitions, for a definition may be taken in the syllogism as a middle term and as part of the major term in the major premise and in the conclusion, causing illusion. That is like our statement: 'Body is substance' and 'Every substance is known to be substance', so 'Body is known to be substance'. Among them: what goes back to transferring the definition, such that it is part of the predicate but we think it is part of the subject, as when it is said: 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or not simple'. For if 'insofar as' were part of the subject, then substance would only be simple and would not be not simple, which is a contradiction. Rather, 'insofar as' whenever it is used in speech in a meaningful way, it is only part of the predicate. That is by prefixing the disjunctive particle on our statement: 'insofar as' and 'from the aspect that' on the two parts of the disjunction, along with prefixing the negation particle on one of the two sides combined with 'insofar as', for it is the counterpart of the affirmative of the two. That is like our statement: 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or it is not, insofar as it is substance, simple.' Among them: that something that is not a definition is inserted into the statement and thought to be a definition, or it is a definition and thought not to be. Example of the first: our statement 'Every animal is a genus for man, and likewise man' — the word 'genus' is inserted only to indicate that it is predicated of man, not that it is essential; hence if you delete it, the meaning does not change, for if you said 'and likewise man' and 'Every man is an animal', it would convey what the first conveyed. As for the example of the second: the word 'known' in our statement: 'Every substance is known to be substance' in the previous example. Were it not for its entry into the major term, the conclusion would be 'Every body is substance', which is exactly the minor premise, so it would be not useful. As for what concerns the form and shape of the proposition: that it is an affirmative with negation, so it is thought to be a simple negative, or negative with modality, so it is thought to be a modal negative, and similarly the reverse in both.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of seven types of errors that arise from the misuse of definitions in logical arguments (صور الحجج). The author, likely Abhari or a commentator, details each pitfall with illustrative examples. The first error occurs when a definition is presented as a single term (مفرد) but the wording is actually compound, like defining 'man' as 'rational animal that takes bread, chews, swallows' instead of simply 'rational animal'. The remedy is to replace it with the proper simple term. The second error relates to ambiguous particles such as 'في' (in) and 'لام' (for) which may indicate either a part-whole relation (جزئية) or a connection (ارتباط). For instance, 'Accident exists in substance' means the accident is in the substance, not that the accident is a part of substance; whereas 'Substance exists for body' means body is a substance. The author advises converting such expressions into clearer forms like 'Body is substance.' He then analyzes four cases where the same particle functions differently in the minor and major premises. A special sub-case concerns negative propositions: whether 'No motion is in motion' is true or false depends on whether 'in' is taken as connection (false) or part-whole (true). The third error is neglecting to state a condition that is implied in the subject or predicate. The fourth is repetition of a term as both middle and part of major term, leading to a trivial conclusion like 'Body is known to be substance' which is essentially the same as the premise. The fifth error is misplacing a term: taking what is part of the predicate as part of the subject, as in the example 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or not', where 'insofar as' must be part of the predicate. The sixth error is inserting a non-definition into the argument (e.g., 'genus' when not essential) or omitting an essential part (e.g., 'known' in the syllogism). The seventh concerns confusion of proposition form: treating an affirmative with negation (موجبة معدولة) as a simple negative, or a modal negative as a negative modality, and vice versa.

Key Terms
شرط
تكرير
حد أوسط
حد أكبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the conclusion 'Body is known to be substance' considered problematic?
A) It is a false statement
B) It does not follow from the premises
C) It is trivial because it essentially repeats the minor premise
D) The definition is incomplete

Key Takeaway
Always ensure definitions are fully stated and not reused in multiple roles that produce trivial conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the syllogism: 'The Qur'an is the speech of Allah. The speech of Allah is uncreated. Therefore the Qur'an is uncreated.' Is the definition of 'speech of Allah' repeated? Could the conclusion be considered trivial? Write a brief analysis.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Pitfalls 5 and 6: Misplacing Definitions and Inserting Non-Definitions
Fifth and sixth pitfalls: misplacement and insertion/omission of definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a definition part is wrongly placed (subject vs. predicate) and when a non-definition is inserted or a definition omitted.

Core Idea
Correct placement of definition components is essential; inserting extraneous or omitting essential parts distorts reasoning.

The fifth pitfall is transferring a definition: a part that belongs to the predicate is mistakenly thought to be part of the subject. The example is 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or not simple' – 'insofar as' must be part of the predicate. The sixth pitfall is inserting a word that is not a definition (like 'genus' in 'Every animal is a genus for man') into the argument, or omitting a necessary definition word (like 'known' in the earlier repetition example).

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase in question
Locate the phrase like 'من حيث هو' (insofar as) or 'جنس' (genus) in the argument.
2
Determine its logical role
Ask whether this phrase is part of the subject or predicate. In the example, 'insofar as' qualifies the predicate, not the subject.
3
Check for extraneous words
See if any word in the argument serves only to clarify the predicate but is mistakenly considered part of the definition. Example: 'genus' in the animal example.
4
Check for omitted essential words
See if a word that is part of the definition (like 'known') has been left out, making the conclusion trivial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثامن في اعتبار أمور نافعة الاعتبار في صور الحجج وأما ما يكون بسبب الحدود فعلى وجوه منها: أن يكون الحد مفرداً، ولا يتحصل كذلك لتركب في لفظه، كما يقال بدل قولنا: (الإنسان يأكل الحيوان الناطق بأخذ الخبز ويتركه في فيه ويمضغه ويبلعه)، فعليك بتبديله بالمفرد فإنه ربما يكون ذلك أسهل. ومنها ما يعود إلى اشتباه الألفاظ الدالة عليها بالأدوات مثل (في كذا ولكذا)، أو حقها أن تكون جزءاً من المحمول ومنها ما يكون بالعكس، مثال الأول قولنا: (العرض) موجود في الجوهر إذ معناه أن الجوهر فيه العرض، لا أنه عرض ومثال الثاني قولنا: (الجوهر موجود للجسم معناه أن الجسم جوهر لا أن الجوهر موجود في الجسم، فعليك أيضاً باعتبار ما هو جزء، وما هو رابطة. وإن نقلت القضية إلى عبارة بدل هذه الأشباه إن أمكن، كما يقال: (الجسم جوهر) بدل قولنا: (الجوهر موجود الجسم)، وربما اتفق أن كانت ما مثل هذه اللفظة دالة في المقدمتين - أعني الصغرى والكبرى - على الجزئية، أو على الارتباط في الصغرى، والجزئية في الكبرى، أو بالعكس، فالأول كما في قولنا: (الكم موجود في الجوهر) و(الاستقامة والانحناء في الكم) فـ (الاستقامة والانحناء في الجوهر)، وأما الثاني، فكما في قولنا: (الجوهر موجود للجسم) و (الجسم موجود للحيوان) فـ (الجوهر موجود للحيوان)، وأما الثالث، فكما في قولنا: (اللون موجود للبياض) و(البياض موجود في الجسم) فـ (اللون) موجود في الجسم، وأما الرابع، فكقولنا: (البياض في الجسم) و(الجسم للحيوان) فـ البياض في الحيوان، بل وربما أمكن مثل ذلك في السوالب كما يقال: (لا شيء من الحركة في الحركة) و(لا شيء من الحركة للحركة)، فإنك إن أخذت حرف في أو اللام دالين على الارتباط كان السلب كاذباً؛ إذ الحركة تصدق عليها أنها حركة لا محالة، فسلب كون الحركة حركة يكون كاذباً، وإن أخذا على أنهما دالان على الجزئية، كان السلب صادقاً؛ إذ ليس يصح القول بوجود الحركة في الحركة، فسلبه يكون صادقاً، فعلى هذا لو قلت (كل انتقال من مكان إلى مكان حركة) و(لا شيء من الحركة في الحركة أو للحركة) وأردت بـ (في) أو (اللام) الجزئية، أنتج نتيجة صادقة أنه ليس المعنى، فإنك لو قلت كذا إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان كان مفيداً لما أفاده الأول، وأما مثال الثاني فكما في لفظ (يُعلم) من قولنا: (وكل جوهر فإنه يعلم أنه جوهر) في المثال السابق، ولولا دخوله في الحد الأكبر، وكذا بالعكس فيهما.
The eighth art: concerning consideration of useful matters for the forms of arguments. As for what is due to definitions, it is in several ways. Among them: that the definition is simple, but it is not obtained as such due to composition in its wording, as when one says instead of our statement: 'Man is a rational animal taking bread, leaving it in his mouth, chewing it, and swallowing it' — so you must replace it with the simple term, for perhaps that is easier. Among them: what goes back to confusion of the particles indicating them, such as 'in so-and-so' and 'for so-and-so', or their proper function is to be part of the predicate, and among them what is the opposite. Example of the first: our statement 'Accident exists in substance', for its meaning is that substance has the accident in it, not that it is an accident. Example of the second: our statement 'Substance exists for body' means that body is a substance, not that substance exists in body. So you must also consider what is part and what is a connective. If you transfer the proposition to an expression instead of these ambiguities if possible, as one says 'Body is substance' instead of our statement 'Substance exists for body'. And it may happen that such a word indicates in both premises — namely the minor and major — part-whole relation, or connection in the minor and part-whole in the major, or the opposite. The first is as in our statement: 'Quantity exists in substance' and 'Straightness and curvature are in quantity', so 'Straightness and curvature are in substance'. The second: as in our statement: 'Substance exists for body' and 'Body exists for animal', so 'Substance exists for animal'. The third: as in our statement: 'Color exists for whiteness' and 'Whiteness exists in body', so 'Color exists in body'. The fourth: as in our statement: 'Whiteness is in body' and 'Body is for animal', so 'Whiteness is in animal'. Indeed, this is also possible in negatives, as one says: 'No motion exists in motion' and 'No motion exists for motion'. If you take the particle 'in' or 'for' as indicating connection, then the negation is false, for motion certainly applies to itself, so negating that motion is motion is false. If they are taken as indicating part-whole, then the negation is true, for it is not correct to say that motion exists in motion, so its negation is true. Thus if you say 'Every transfer from place to place is motion' and 'No motion is in or for motion' and you intend by 'in' or 'for' the part-whole relation, it yields a true conclusion: that the transfer to a place has no motion or has no motion in it. Otherwise, the conclusion is false. Among them: what goes back to neglecting to state a condition that is actually a part taken with the subject or predicate or external, as previously defined. Among them: what goes back to repetition of definitions, for a definition may be taken in the syllogism as a middle term and as part of the major term in the major premise and in the conclusion, causing illusion. That is like our statement: 'Body is substance' and 'Every substance is known to be substance', so 'Body is known to be substance'. Among them: what goes back to transferring the definition, such that it is part of the predicate but we think it is part of the subject, as when it is said: 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or not simple'. For if 'insofar as' were part of the subject, then substance would only be simple and would not be not simple, which is a contradiction. Rather, 'insofar as' whenever it is used in speech in a meaningful way, it is only part of the predicate. That is by prefixing the disjunctive particle on our statement: 'insofar as' and 'from the aspect that' on the two parts of the disjunction, along with prefixing the negation particle on one of the two sides combined with 'insofar as', for it is the counterpart of the affirmative of the two. That is like our statement: 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or it is not, insofar as it is substance, simple.' Among them: that something that is not a definition is inserted into the statement and thought to be a definition, or it is a definition and thought not to be. Example of the first: our statement 'Every animal is a genus for man, and likewise man' — the word 'genus' is inserted only to indicate that it is predicated of man, not that it is essential; hence if you delete it, the meaning does not change, for if you said 'and likewise man' and 'Every man is an animal', it would convey what the first conveyed. As for the example of the second: the word 'known' in our statement: 'Every substance is known to be substance' in the previous example. Were it not for its entry into the major term, the conclusion would be 'Every body is substance', which is exactly the minor premise, so it would be not useful. As for what concerns the form and shape of the proposition: that it is an affirmative with negation, so it is thought to be a simple negative, or negative with modality, so it is thought to be a modal negative, and similarly the reverse in both.

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of seven types of errors that arise from the misuse of definitions in logical arguments (صور الحجج). The author, likely Abhari or a commentator, details each pitfall with illustrative examples. The first error occurs when a definition is presented as a single term (مفرد) but the wording is actually compound, like defining 'man' as 'rational animal that takes bread, chews, swallows' instead of simply 'rational animal'. The remedy is to replace it with the proper simple term. The second error relates to ambiguous particles such as 'في' (in) and 'لام' (for) which may indicate either a part-whole relation (جزئية) or a connection (ارتباط). For instance, 'Accident exists in substance' means the accident is in the substance, not that the accident is a part of substance; whereas 'Substance exists for body' means body is a substance. The author advises converting such expressions into clearer forms like 'Body is substance.' He then analyzes four cases where the same particle functions differently in the minor and major premises. A special sub-case concerns negative propositions: whether 'No motion is in motion' is true or false depends on whether 'in' is taken as connection (false) or part-whole (true). The third error is neglecting to state a condition that is implied in the subject or predicate. The fourth is repetition of a term as both middle and part of major term, leading to a trivial conclusion like 'Body is known to be substance' which is essentially the same as the premise. The fifth error is misplacing a term: taking what is part of the predicate as part of the subject, as in the example 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or not', where 'insofar as' must be part of the predicate. The sixth error is inserting a non-definition into the argument (e.g., 'genus' when not essential) or omitting an essential part (e.g., 'known' in the syllogism). The seventh concerns confusion of proposition form: treating an affirmative with negation (موجبة معدولة) as a simple negative, or a modal negative as a negative modality, and vice versa.

Why does the author say the word 'genus' in 'Every animal is a genus for man' can be deleted without changing the meaning?
A) Because 'genus' is a technical term not needed in ordinary language
B) Because 'genus' is not part of the essential definition of man
C) Because 'man' already implies genus
D) Because the statement is false

Key Takeaway
Each part of a definition has a specific logical place; extraneous insertions and omissions alter the argument's validity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'A hadith, as it is a hadith, is either mutawatir or ahad.' Identify the phrase 'as it is a hadith' and explain whether it belongs to the subject or predicate. Then rewrite the statement correctly.

Pitfall 7: Confusion of Proposition Forms (Modal and Negated Propositions)
Seventh pitfall: confusion of proposition form, especially modal and negated propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an affirmative with negation (موجبة معدولة) and a simple negative, and between modal negative and negative modality.

Core Idea
The form of a proposition—negation placement and modality—affects its logical interpretation and truth conditions.

The seventh pitfall concerns the form and shape of the proposition. An affirmative proposition with negation (e.g., 'Man is non-animal') can be mistaken for a simple negative ('No man is animal'). Similarly, a modal negative (e.g., 'It is possible that not') differs from the negation of a modal (e.g., 'It is not possible'). Confusing these leads to invalid inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition's surface form
Look at the words: does the proposition have a negation particle, a modality, or both?
2
Classify the proposition type
Determine if it is: simple affirmative, simple negative, affirmative with negation (موجبة معدولة), modal affirmative, modal negative, or negative modality.
3
Compare with what it might be confused with
Check if the proposition could be misinterpreted as another type. Example: 'Man is non-animal' is affirmative with negation, not a simple negative.
4
Assess the logical implications
Understand that these forms have different truth conditions and behave differently in syllogisms. A mistake in classification leads to wrong conclusions.

وإلا كانت النتيجة فـ (كل جسم جوهر) وهي عين المقدمة الصغرى، فلا يكون مفيداً. وأما ما يرجع إلى شكل المقدمة وصورتها، بأن تكون موجبة معدولة، فيظن أنها سالبة بسيطة، أو سالبة للجهة، فيظن أنها جهة سالبة، The eighth art: concerning consideration of useful matters for the forms of arguments. As for what is due to definitions, it is in several ways. Among them: that the definition is simple, but it is not obtained as such due to composition in its wording, as when one says instead of our statement: 'Man is a rational animal taking bread, leaving it in his mouth, chewing it, and swallowing it' — so you must replace it with the simple term, for perhaps that is easier. Among them: what goes back to confusion of the particles indicating them, such as 'in so-and-so' and 'for so-and-so', or their proper function is to be part of the predicate, and among them what is the opposite. Example of the first: our statement 'Accident exists in substance', for its meaning is that substance has the accident in it, not that it is an accident. Example of the second: our statement 'Substance exists for body' means that body is a substance, not that substance exists in body. So you must also consider what is part and what is a connective. If you transfer the proposition to an expression instead of these ambiguities if possible, as one says 'Body is substance' instead of our statement 'Substance exists for body'. And it may happen that such a word indicates in both premises — namely the minor and major — part-whole relation, or connection in the minor and part-whole in the major, or the opposite. The first is as in our statement: 'Quantity exists in substance' and 'Straightness and curvature are in quantity', so 'Straightness and curvature are in substance'. The second: as in our statement: 'Substance exists for body' and 'Body exists for animal', so 'Substance exists for animal'. The third: as in our statement: 'Color exists for whiteness' and 'Whiteness exists in body', so 'Color exists in body'. The fourth: as in our statement: 'Whiteness is in body' and 'Body is for animal', so 'Whiteness is in animal'. Indeed, this is also possible in negatives, as one says: 'No motion exists in motion' and 'No motion exists for motion'. If you take the particle 'in' or 'for' as indicating connection, then the negation is false, for motion certainly applies to itself, so negating that motion is motion is false. If they are taken as indicating part-whole, then the negation is true, for it is not correct to say that motion exists in motion, so its negation is true. Thus if you say 'Every transfer from place to place is motion' and 'No motion is in or for motion' and you intend by 'in' or 'for' the part-whole relation, it yields a true conclusion: that the transfer to a place has no motion or has no motion in it. Otherwise, the conclusion is false. Among them: what goes back to neglecting to state a condition that is actually a part taken with the subject or predicate or external, as previously defined. Among them: what goes back to repetition of definitions, for a definition may be taken in the syllogism as a middle term and as part of the major term in the major premise and in the conclusion, causing illusion. That is like our statement: 'Body is substance' and 'Every substance is known to be substance', so 'Body is known to be substance'. Among them: what goes back to transferring the definition, such that it is part of the predicate but we think it is part of the subject, as when it is said: 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or not simple'. For if 'insofar as' were part of the subject, then substance would only be simple and would not be not simple, which is a contradiction. Rather, 'insofar as' whenever it is used in speech in a meaningful way, it is only part of the predicate. That is by prefixing the disjunctive particle on our statement: 'insofar as' and 'from the aspect that' on the two parts of the disjunction, along with prefixing the negation particle on one of the two sides combined with 'insofar as', for it is the counterpart of the affirmative of the two. That is like our statement: 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or it is not, insofar as it is substance, simple.' Among them: that something that is not a definition is inserted into the statement and thought to be a definition, or it is a definition and thought not to be. Example of the first: our statement 'Every animal is a genus for man, and likewise man' — the word 'genus' is inserted only to indicate that it is predicated of man, not that it is essential; hence if you delete it, the meaning does not change, for if you said 'and likewise man' and 'Every man is an animal', it would convey what the first conveyed. As for the example of the second: the word 'known' in our statement: 'Every substance is known to be substance' in the previous example. Were it not for its entry into the major term, the conclusion would be 'Every body is substance', which is exactly the minor premise, so it would be not useful. As for what concerns the form and shape of the proposition: that it is an affirmative with negation, so it is thought to be a simple negative, or negative with modality, so it is thought to be a modal negative, and similarly the reverse in both.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of seven types of errors that arise from the misuse of definitions in logical arguments (صور الحجج). The author, likely Abhari or a commentator, details each pitfall with illustrative examples. The first error occurs when a definition is presented as a single term (مفرد) but the wording is actually compound, like defining 'man' as 'rational animal that takes bread, chews, swallows' instead of simply 'rational animal'. The remedy is to replace it with the proper simple term. The second error relates to ambiguous particles such as 'في' (in) and 'لام' (for) which may indicate either a part-whole relation (جزئية) or a connection (ارتباط). For instance, 'Accident exists in substance' means the accident is in the substance, not that the accident is a part of substance; whereas 'Substance exists for body' means body is a substance. The author advises converting such expressions into clearer forms like 'Body is substance.' He then analyzes four cases where the same particle functions differently in the minor and major premises. A special sub-case concerns negative propositions: whether 'No motion is in motion' is true or false depends on whether 'in' is taken as connection (false) or part-whole (true). The third error is neglecting to state a condition that is implied in the subject or predicate. The fourth is repetition of a term as both middle and part of major term, leading to a trivial conclusion like 'Body is known to be substance' which is essentially the same as the premise. The fifth error is misplacing a term: taking what is part of the predicate as part of the subject, as in the example 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or not', where 'insofar as' must be part of the predicate. The sixth error is inserting a non-definition into the argument (e.g., 'genus' when not essential) or omitting an essential part (e.g., 'known' in the syllogism). The seventh concerns confusion of proposition form: treating an affirmative with negation (موجبة معدولة) as a simple negative, or a modal negative as a negative modality, and vice versa.

Key Terms
موجبة معدولة
سالبة بسيطة
سالبة للجهة
جهة سالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They are exactly the same
B) The former is negative, the latter is affirmative with a negated predicate
C) The former has a modal, the latter does not
D) The former is universal, the latter is particular

Key Takeaway
Always check the exact form of a proposition: an affirmative with negation is not a simple negative, and modal forms must be distinguished from negated modalities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider these two propositions: 'No human is immortal' (simple negative) and 'Every human is mortal' (affirmative with negation? Actually 'mortal' is not a negation, but consider 'Every human is non-immortal' which is affirmative with negation). Fallacy of Neglecting to State a Condition
First fallacy: neglecting to state a condition with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a case where a condition is omitted in a syllogism and explain how it leads to a false conclusion.

Core Idea
A hidden condition can make a valid-looking argument false.

The author begins with an example involving the prepositions 'fi' and the partitive lam, which are conditions that must be stated explicitly. If omitted, the conclusion may be false. This fallacy is called neglecting to state a condition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the example involving 'fi' or 'lam'
Find the part of the Arabic where the author discusses 'fi' and the partitive lam. This example illustrates the fallacy.
2
Identify the omitted condition
What condition is implied but not stated? The author says it is a part taken with the subject or predicate.
3
See how omission leads to false conclusion
Without the condition, the conclusion appears true but is actually false. Understand the role of conditions in syllogisms.
