تتمة المقالة الرابعة في تعريف صور الحجج من مكان وأردت بـ (في) أو (اللام الجزئية، أنتج نتيجة صادقة أنه ليس للانتقال إلى مكان حركة أو ليس فيه (حركة)، وإلا فالنتيجة تكون كاذبة. ومنها ما يعود إلى إهمال التصريح بشرط، هو في الحقيقة جزء مأخوذ مع الموضوع، أو المحمول، أو خارج، كما سبق تعريفه. ومنها ما يعود إلى تكرار الحدود، فإنه ربما كان الحد مأخوذاً في القياس على أنه حد أوسط، وعلى جزء من الحد الأكبر في المقدمة الكبرى وفي النتيجة فيوقع الوهم، وذلك كقولنا: (الجسم (جوهر) وكل جوهر فإنه يُعلم أنه (جوهر) فـ (الجسم يعلم أنه جوهر). ومنها ما يعود إلى نقل الحد، بأن يكون جزءاً من المحمول، ونظن أنه جزء من الموضوع، كما يقال: (الجوهر من حيث هو جوهر إما بسيط وإما لا بسيط) فـ (من حيث هو لو كانت جزءاً من الموضوع لما كان الجوهر إلا بسيطاً ولما كان لا بسيطاً وهو خلف، بل من حيث هو مهما دلت في الكلام على وجه مفيد لا يكون إلا جزءاً من المحمول، وذلك بأن يقدم حرف (إما وإما على قولنا: (من حيث هو) ومن جهة ما هو على جزئي الانفصال، مع تقديم حرف السلب على أحد الطرفين مقترناً به من حيث هو، إذ هو المقابل للموجب منهما، وذلك كقولنا: (الجوهر إما من حيث هو جوهر بسيط وإما أنه لا من حيث هو جوهر بسيط). ومنها: أن يدرج في القول ما ليس بحد ويظن أنه حد، أو أنه حد، ويظن أنه ليس بحد مثال الأول قولنا كل الحيوان جنس للإنسان وكذا إنسان فإن لفظ الجنس إنما أدرج في القول لبيان أنه محمول على الإنسان لا أنه ضروري؛ ولهذا لو حذفته لم يتغير المعنى، فإنك لو قلت كذا إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان كان مفيداً لما أفاده الأول، وأما مثال الثاني فكما في لفظ ( يُعلم) من قولنا: (وكل جوهر فإنه يعلم أنه جوهر) في المثال السابق، ولولا دخوله في الحد الأكبر، وإلا كانت النتيجة فـ (كل جسم (جوهر) وهي عين المقدمة الصغرى، فلا يكون مفيداً.
Completion of the fourth article on defining the forms of arguments. From a place, and I intended with 'fi' or the partitive lam, it produced a true conclusion that there is no motion for transition to a place or there is no motion in it, otherwise the conclusion would be false. Among them is that which pertains to neglecting to state a condition, which in reality is a part taken with the subject, or the predicate, or external, as previously defined. Among them is that which pertains to repetition of terms, for perhaps the term is taken in the syllogism as a middle term, and as part of the major term in the major premise and in the conclusion, causing illusion, such as our saying: 'The body is a substance, and every substance is known to be a substance, therefore the body is known to be a substance.' Among them is that which pertains to transfer of the term, in that it is part of the predicate, and we think it is part of the subject, as is said: 'Substance insofar as it is substance is either simple or not simple.' So 'insofar as' if it were part of the subject, substance would only be simple and would not be not simple, which is a contradiction. Rather, 'insofar as' whenever it indicates in speech a beneficial aspect, it is only part of the predicate, and that is by placing the letter 'either-or' before our saying 'insofar as' and 'from the aspect of' on the two parts of the disjunction, with the placement of the negation letter on one of the two sides connected to 'insofar as', since it is the opposite of the affirmative of the two, such as our saying: 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or it is not insofar as it is substance simple.' Among them is that which pertains to inserting into the statement what is not a limit and thinking it is a limit, or it is a limit and thinking it is not a limit. Example of the first is our saying: 'Every animal is a genus for man, and likewise man.' For the word 'genus' is only inserted into the statement to indicate that it is predicated of man, not that it is necessary; therefore if you omitted it, the meaning would not change. For if you said 'likewise man and every man is an animal', it would be as useful as the first. As for the example of the second, it is like the word 'is known' from our saying: 'And every substance is known to be a substance' in the previous example. If it had not entered into the major term, the conclusion would be 'Every body is a substance', which is exactly the minor premise, so it would not be useful.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage defines four types of fallacies that occur in syllogistic reasoning, specifically related to the terms of the syllogism. The author begins with an example illustrating a fallacy involving the preposition 'fi' or the partitive lam, then systematically enumerates each type: First, neglecting to state a condition: the fallacy occurs when a condition that is actually part of the subject or predicate is omitted, leading to a false conclusion. Second, repetition of terms: when the same term is used as the middle term and also as part of the major term in the major premise and conclusion, causing confusion. The example given is: 'The body is a substance. Every substance is known to be a substance. Therefore the body is known to be a substance.' Here 'substance' appears as middle term and also in the predicate of the conclusion. Third, transfer of the term: when a term that is part of the predicate is mistakenly thought to be part of the subject. The author uses the example of 'Substance insofar as it is substance is either simple or not simple.' Here 'insofar as' (min haythu huwa) is properly part of the predicate, not the subject. If it were part of the subject, the statement would be contradictory. The correct expression is with 'either-or' and negation. Fourth, insertion of what is not a limit: Two sub-cases: (a) inserting a word that is not a limit (like 'genus' in 'Every animal is a genus for man') where it is not necessary for the meaning, and (b) using a word that is a limit but thinking it is not (like 'is known' in the earlier example), which makes the conclusion redundant. Each fallacy is illustrated with an example from logic (using terms like substance, body, animal, man). The passage emphasizes that these errors deceive in the analysis of syllogisms.

Key Terms
إهمال التصريح بشرط (ihmal al-tasrih bi-shart): Neglecting to state a condition; omitting a condition that is part of the subject or predicate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Inserting an unnecessary word
D) Shifting a term from predicate to subject

Key Takeaway
Neglecting to state a condition that is integral to the subject or predicate can make a syllogism invalid.

Fallacy of Repetition of Terms
Second fallacy: repetition of terms with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect repetition of the same term as middle term and part of the major term in a syllogism.

Core Idea
Repeating a term creates an illusion of deduction without real progress.

The author gives an example: 'The body is a substance, every substance is known to be a substance, therefore the body is known to be a substance.' Here 'substance' appears as the middle term and also in the predicate of the conclusion, making the conclusion merely restate the premise.

Guided Path
1
Write the syllogism in standard form
Put the example into major premise, minor premise, conclusion format.
2
Identify the middle term
Which term is repeated? In this case, 'substance' appears in both premises.
3
Notice the repetition in the conclusion
The conclusion contains the same term 'substance' as part of the predicate, making it redundant.
4
Understand why it's invalid
A valid syllogism should introduce new information; here the conclusion is essentially the minor premise with an added illusion.

تتمة المقالة الرابعة في تعريف صور الحجج من مكان وأردت بـ (في) أو (اللام الجزئية، أنتج نتيجة صادقة أنه ليس للانتقال إلى مكان حركة أو ليس فيه (حركة)، وإلا فالنتيجة تكون كاذبة. ومنها ما يعود إلى إهمال التصريح بشرط، هو في الحقيقة جزء مأخوذ مع الموضوع، أو المحمول، أو خارج، كما سبق تعريفه. ومنها ما يعود إلى تكرار الحدود، فإنه ربما كان الحد مأخوذاً في القياس على أنه حد أوسط، وعلى جزء من الحد الأكبر في المقدمة الكبرى وفي النتيجة فيوقع الوهم، وذلك كقولنا: (الجسم (جوهر) وكل جوهر فإنه يُعلم أنه (جوهر) فـ (الجسم يعلم أنه جوهر). ومنها ما يعود إلى نقل الحد، بأن يكون جزءاً من المحمول، ونظن أنه جزء من الموضوع، كما يقال: (الجوهر من حيث هو جوهر إما بسيط وإما لا بسيط) فـ (من حيث هو لو كانت جزءاً من الموضوع لما كان الجوهر إلا بسيطاً ولما كان لا بسيطاً وهو خلف، بل من حيث هو مهما دلت في الكلام على وجه مفيد لا يكون إلا جزءاً من المحمول، وذلك بأن يقدم حرف (إما وإما على قولنا: (من حيث هو) ومن جهة ما هو على جزئي الانفصال، مع تقديم حرف السلب على أحد الطرفين مقترناً به من حيث هو، إذ هو المقابل للموجب منهما، وذلك كقولنا: (الجوهر إما من حيث هو جوهر بسيط وإما أنه لا من حيث هو جوهر بسيط). ومنها: أن يدرج في القول ما ليس بحد ويظن أنه حد، أو أنه حد، ويظن أنه ليس بحد مثال الأول قولنا كل الحيوان جنس للإنسان وكذا إنسان فإن لفظ الجنس إنما أدرج في القول لبيان أنه محمول على الإنسان لا أنه ضروري؛ ولهذا لو حذفته لم يتغير المعنى، فإنك لو قلت كذا إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان كان مفيداً لما أفاده الأول، وأما مثال الثاني فكما في لفظ ( يُعلم) من قولنا: (وكل جوهر فإنه يعلم أنه جوهر) في المثال السابق، ولولا دخوله في الحد الأكبر، وإلا كانت النتيجة فـ (كل جسم (جوهر) وهي عين المقدمة الصغرى، فلا يكون مفيداً.
Completion of the fourth article on defining the forms of arguments. From a place, and I intended with 'fi' or the partitive lam, it produced a true conclusion that there is no motion for transition to a place or there is no motion in it, otherwise the conclusion would be false. Among them is that which pertains to neglecting to state a condition, which in reality is a part taken with the subject, or the predicate, or external, as previously defined. Among them is that which pertains to repetition of terms, for perhaps the term is taken in the syllogism as a middle term, and as part of the major term in the major premise and in the conclusion, causing illusion, such as our saying: 'The body is a substance, and every substance is known to be a substance, therefore the body is known to be a substance.' Among them is that which pertains to transfer of the term, in that it is part of the predicate, and we think it is part of the subject, as is said: 'Substance insofar as it is substance is either simple or not simple.' So 'insofar as' if it were part of the subject, substance would only be simple and would not be not simple, which is a contradiction. Rather, 'insofar as' whenever it indicates in speech a beneficial aspect, it is only part of the predicate, and that is by placing the letter 'either-or' before our saying 'insofar as' and 'from the aspect of' on the two parts of the disjunction, with the placement of the negation letter on one of the two sides connected to 'insofar as', since it is the opposite of the affirmative of the two, such as our saying: 'Substance, insofar as it is substance, is either simple or it is not insofar as it is substance simple.' Among them is that which pertains to inserting into the statement what is not a limit and thinking it is a limit, or it is a limit and thinking it is not a limit. Example of the first is our saying: 'Every animal is a genus for man, and likewise man.' For the word 'genus' is only inserted into the statement to indicate that it is predicated of man, not that it is necessary; therefore if you omitted it, the meaning would not change. For if you said 'likewise man and every man is an animal', it would be as useful as the first. As for the example of the second, it is like the word 'is known' from our saying: 'And every substance is known to be a substance' in the previous example. If it had not entered into the major term, the conclusion would be 'Every body is a substance', which is exactly the minor premise, so it would not be useful.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage defines four types of fallacies that occur in syllogistic reasoning, specifically related to the terms of the syllogism. The author begins with an example illustrating a fallacy involving the preposition 'fi' or the partitive lam, then systematically enumerates each type: First, neglecting to state a condition: the fallacy occurs when a condition that is actually part of the subject or predicate is omitted, leading to a false conclusion. Second, repetition of terms: when the same term is used as the middle term and also as part of the major term in the major premise and conclusion, causing confusion. The example given is: 'The body is a substance. Every substance is known to be a substance. Therefore the body is known to be a substance.' Here 'substance' appears as middle term and also in the predicate of the conclusion. Third, transfer of the term: when a term that is part of the predicate is mistakenly thought to be part of the subject. The author uses the example of 'Substance insofar as it is substance is either simple or not simple.' Here 'insofar as' (min haythu huwa) is properly part of the predicate, not the subject. If it were part of the subject, the statement would be contradictory. The correct expression is with 'either-or' and negation. Fourth, insertion of what is not a limit: Two sub-cases: (a) inserting a word that is not a limit (like 'genus' in 'Every animal is a genus for man') where it is not necessary for the meaning, and (b) using a word that is a limit but thinking it is not (like 'is known' in the earlier example), which makes the conclusion redundant. Each fallacy is illustrated with an example from logic (using terms like substance, body, animal, man). The passage emphasizes that these errors deceive in the analysis of syllogisms.

Key Terms
تكرار الحدود (tikrar al-hudud): Repetition of terms; using the same term as both the middle term and part of the major term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main issue in the fallacy of repetition of terms?
A) A condition is omitted
B) A term is used as both middle and major term
C) A term is transferred from predicate to subject
D) An unnecessary word is inserted

Key Takeaway
Repetition of the middle term as part of the major term produces an illusion of deduction without genuine inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar invalid syllogism on a fiqh topic where a term is repeated. Then correct it to a valid syllogism.

Fallacy of Transfer of the Term
Third fallacy: transfer of the term with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a term that belongs to the predicate is mistakenly moved to the subject.

Core Idea
Shifting a term from predicate to subject changes the logical structure and can lead to contradiction.

The author uses the example of 'substance insofar as it is substance is either simple or not simple.' He explains that 'insofar as' (min haythu huwa) is part of the predicate, not the subject. If it were in the subject, the statement would be contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the example carefully
The example is: 'Substance insofar as it is substance is either simple or not simple.' Identify the parts.
2
Understand the incorrect placement
If 'insofar as' is treated as part of the subject, it leads to a contradiction because the subject would be limited.
3
Identify the correct placement
The author shows that 'insofar as' should be part of the predicate, and expresses it with 'either-or' and negation.

Fallacy of Insertion of What is Not a Limit
Fourth fallacy: insertion of non-limit with two examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential and non-essential terms in a syllogism and identify when a non-limit is inserted or a necessary limit is omitted.

Core Idea
Inserting unnecessary terms or omitting necessary ones undermines the purpose of the argument.

The author gives two sub-cases: (1) inserting a word that is not a limit and thinking it is, e.g., 'genus' in 'Every animal is a genus for man', which can be omitted without changing meaning; (2) using a word that is a limit but thinking it is not, e.g., 'is known' in the earlier example, without which the conclusion would be trivial.

Guided Path
1
Read the first example (insertion of 'genus')
The statement 'Every animal is a genus for man and likewise man' contains 'genus' which is not necessary.
2
Remove the inserted term and check meaning
If you remove 'genus', the statement 'likewise man and every man is an animal' conveys the same information.
3
Read the second example (omission of 'is known')
In the earlier syllogism, if 'is known' is not part of the major term, the conclusion would be 'Every body is a substance', which is exactly the minor premise.
4
Understand the two sub-cases
The first inserts a superfluous term; the second omits a necessary term, making the argument redundant.

Fallacies in Premise Form: Modified Affirmatives and Modal Confusions
Fallacies from premise form misinterpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a modified affirmative from a simple negative and a negative of modality from a modal negative by analyzing the placement of negation.

Core Idea
The location of negation changes whether a proposition is affirmative or negative.

In logical analysis, the form of a premise can trick you. A premise that says 'Every A is non-B' is still affirmative in mood, but it is often mistaken for a simple negative like 'No A is B'. Similarly, modal statements can be misread: 'It is not necessary that A is B' differs from 'It is necessary that A is not B'. Recognizing these distinctions prevents errors when evaluating arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position of negation
Locate the word 'not' (or its equivalent) in the premise. If it negates the predicate (e.g., 'is not B') while the overall judgment is affirmative (e.g., 'every A'), the premise is a modified affirmative (mujaba mu'addala).
2
Compare with a simple negative
A simple negative (saliba basita) has the negation at the copula: 'No A is B' (laysa shay' min A bi-B). Check if the negation affects the entire relation between subject and predicate.
3
Examine modality
If the premise includes a modal term (wujub, imkan, etc.), note where the negation attaches. In 'saliba li'l-jiha', the modal operator is negated (e.g., 'laysa wajib an'). In 'jiha saliba', the modal operator includes negation (e.g., 'wajib an laysa').
4
Apply to a real example
Take a premise from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is not permissible' (kull muskir ghayr ja'iz). This is a modified affirmative. It is not equivalent to 'No intoxicant is permissible' (a simple negative), though they are logically equivalent in extension. Understand the nuance.
The fallacy lies in treating the modified affirmative as if it were a simple negative in terms of conversion rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثامن في اعتبار أمور نافعة الاعتبار في صور الحجج وأما ما يرجع إلى شكل المقدمة وصورتها، بأن تكون موجبة معدولة، فيظن أنها سالبة بسيطة، أو سالبة للجهة، فيظن أنها جهة سالبة، وكذا بالعكس فيهما. وأما ما يرجع إلى شكل القياس، فبأن يكون مركباً على ما أسلفناه، فهذه جملة مما يغتر به في تحليل القياس، والأقوال الجارية بين الناس، ينتفع بمعرفتها في التحليل وتسهيله وقلّما يخلو قياس من بعضها.
The eighth art on considering beneficial matters in the forms of arguments. And as for what pertains to the form and shape of the premise, such that it is a modified affirmative, and it is thought to be a simple negative, or a negative of modality, and it is thought to be a negative modality, and likewise the opposite in both. And as for what pertains to the form of the syllogism, that it is compound as we have previously mentioned. These are a summary of what is deceived by in the analysis of syllogisms and common statements among people; knowledge of them is beneficial in analysis and its facilitation, and rarely does a syllogism lack some of them.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage, which begins a new section (the eighth art), discusses two additional types of fallacies in syllogistic reasoning. First, fallacies related to the form of the premise: a premise may be a 'modified affirmative' (mujaba mu'addala), which is an affirmative proposition that contains a negation (e.g., 'Every A is not-B'), and it is mistakenly thought to be a simple negative (e.g., 'No A is B'). Also, a premise may be negative with respect to modality, e.g., 'It is not necessary that A is B,' and it is confused with a modal negative, e.g., 'It is necessary that A is not B.' The same confusion occurs in reverse. Second, fallacies related to the form of the syllogism itself: the syllogism may be compound (murakkab), meaning it consists of multiple syllogisms chained together. Such compound arguments require careful analysis to avoid deception. The author concludes that these are a summary of the things that deceive people in analyzing syllogisms and everyday statements. Knowledge of these fallacies is useful for analysis and is rarely absent from any syllogism.

موجبة معدولة
سالبة بسيطة
سالبة للجهة
جهة سالبة

The Fallacy of Compound Syllogisms
Fallacy from compound syllogism structure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to break down a compound syllogism into its component simple syllogisms and verify each step's validity.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism is a chain of inferences; a break in any link invalidates the whole.

Not every argument is a single syllogism. Sometimes multiple syllogisms are linked together. The author warns that such compound syllogisms (qiyas murakkab) can be deceptive if not analyzed step by step. Learning to decompose them is essential for rigorous logical evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall conclusion
Determine the final proposition the argument aims to prove. Work backwards to see how it is derived.
2
Locate intermediate conclusions
Look for statements that are not explicit premises but appear to be conclusions of sub-arguments. These are the links in the chain.
3
Separate into simple syllogisms
Each simple syllogism consists of two premises and one conclusion. Arrange the argument as a sequence: Premises → Intermediate Conclusion 1 → Premises → Final Conclusion, etc.
Each step must have exactly three terms.
4
Check each sub-syllogism for validity
Apply the rules of categorical syllogisms (figure, mood, distribution) to each component. If any sub-syllogism is invalid, the entire compound is unsound.

Source Text

Accidental Conclusions from Categorical Syllogisms
Author explains how syllogisms produce accidental conclusions beyond their essential result.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the accidental conclusions that follow from a categorical syllogism's essential conclusion based on its quantity and quality.

Core Idea
The essential conclusion is the key; accidental conclusions are its logical extensions, not extra premises.

A categorical syllogism directly yields one essential conclusion. However, from that conclusion, other propositions can be inferred 'accidentally' — like the converse or contrapositive, or conclusions about subclasses. This lesson unpacks the four cases of essential conclusions and what follows from each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the essential conclusion's type
Determine whether the essential conclusion is universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative.
This classification is crucial because each type has different accidental consequences.
2
Apply the rules for each type
For universal affirmative: accidental conclusions include the simple converse, contrapositive, and judgments about terms sharing the minor term. For universal negative: similar but irrespective of figure. For particular affirmative: same except subsumption under the minor term is excluded. For particular negative: no converse or contrapositive; only a judgment about terms sharing the relation to the middle term.
Memorize the pattern: UA and UN have the most accidental conclusions; PA loses subsumption; PN loses converse and contrapositive entirely.
3
Distinguish direct from indirect accidents
Some accidental conclusions (like converse and contrapositive) follow directly from the essential conclusion. Others (like the conclusion for everything under the minor term) are 'consequent of a consequent' (lāzim al-lāzim), not directly from the syllogism.
This distinction prevents overestimating what the syllogism itself proves.
4
Recognize exceptions for particular conclusions
For particular affirmative, subsumption under the minor term is not possible because the conclusion is particular. For particular negative, neither converse nor contrapositive are defined, so only a weaker accidental conclusion applies.
The author emphasizes these exceptions to avoid invalid inferences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع
في استفزاز النتائج
نقول: كل قياس أنتج نتيجة بالذات فقد ينتج نتائج أخرى بالعرض، ويختلف ذلك بحسب اختلاف القياس من الاقتراني والاستثنائي ونتيجته، فإن كان القياس اقترانياً فنتيجته إما كليّة موجبة أو سالبة، وإما جزئية موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت نتيجة الاقتران بالذات كلية موجبة فما يلزمه بالعرض ما هو نتيجة مع نتيجة، ومنه ما هو نتيجة تحت نتيجة، فالأول كالعكس المستوي للنتيجة، وعكس النقيض كما عرف، وكما في لزوم النتيجة في كل ما هو مشارك للحد الأصغر في دخوله تحت الحد الأوسط، والثاني فكلزومها في كل ما هو داخل تحت الحد الأصغر. وعلى هذا تكون النتائج العرضية، إن كانت نتيجة الاقتران كليّة سالبة، من غير مبالاة بكون الاقتران من الشكل الأول أو الثاني، أو الرابع، وكذا إن كانت جزئية موجبة ما عدا استتباع ما تحت الحد الأصغر للنتيجة؛ لأن استتباعه في الحقيقة ليس يمكن إلا مع فرض النتيجة كبرى وفرض ما تحتها بالنسبة إليها صغرى وذلك غير مفيد هاهنا؛ لكونها جزئية وما تحتها جزئي.
وأما إن كانت النتيجة جزئية سالبة، فهي لا تستتبع العكس ولا عكس النقيض؛ إذ ليس هو لها، ولا ما هو داخل تحتها، لما حققناه في الجزئية الموجبة، بل إنما تستتبع مثل حكمها في ما هو مشارك لها في النسبة إلى الحد الأوسط، فلزوم العكس وعكس النقيض ليس عن نفس القياس، وإنما هو لازم اللازم ولزوم النتيجة في ما هو داخل تحت الحد الأصغر، وفي ما هو مساءٍ له في نسبته إلى الحد الأوسط، وإن كان لزومه ظاهراً عند ظهور النتيجة، فليس عن نفس القياس المذكور بالذات، ولا عن نفس النتيجة بالذات أيضاً، بل الأول إنما يلزم بالذات عن النتيجة،
The seventh chapter: On deriving conclusions. We say: Every syllogism that produces a conclusion essentially may produce other conclusions accidentally, and this varies according to the difference of the syllogism — categorical or conditional — and its conclusion. If the syllogism is categorical, its conclusion is either universal affirmative or negative, or particular affirmative or negative. If the essential conclusion of the categorical is universal affirmative, then what follows from it accidentally is either a conclusion alongside the conclusion, or a conclusion subordinate to the conclusion. The first is like the simple converse of the conclusion, and the contrapositive as known, and like the necessity of the conclusion for everything that shares the minor term in falling under the middle term. The second is like its necessity for everything that falls under the minor term. And upon this are the accidental conclusions. If the categorical conclusion is universal negative, without regard to whether the categorical is of the first, second, or fourth figure. Similarly if it is particular affirmative, except for the subsumption of what is under the minor term under the conclusion, because its subsumption in reality is only possible by supposing the conclusion as a major premise and supposing what is under it as a minor premise relative to it, and that is not useful here because it is particular and what is under it is particular. As for if the conclusion is particular negative, it does not entail the converse nor the contrapositive, since it does not have those, nor what falls under it, as we have verified in the particular affirmative. Rather it only entails something like its judgment regarding what shares with it in relation to the middle term. So the entailment of the converse and contrapositive is not from the syllogism itself, but rather it is a consequent of a consequent. And the entailment of the conclusion for what falls under the minor term, and for what is equal to it in its relation to the middle term, even if its entailment is apparent when the conclusion appears, it is not from the syllogism mentioned essentially, nor from the conclusion itself essentially either. Rather, the first is essentially entailed by the conclusion.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on accidental conclusions (نتائج بالعرض) that follow from categorical syllogisms. The author begins by stating a general principle: any syllogism that essentially (بالذات) produces a conclusion may also accidentally produce other conclusions, and this depends on the type of syllogism (categorical or conditional) and the nature of its essential conclusion. The focus here is on categorical syllogisms. The author then categorizes the essential conclusion of a categorical syllogism into four types based on quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). For each type, he explains what accidental conclusions follow. First, if the essential conclusion is universal affirmative, two kinds of accidental conclusions arise: (1) conclusions that are 'alongside' the conclusion, such as the simple converse (عكس مستوي) and the contrapositive (عكس النقيض), and also the necessity of the conclusion for everything that shares the minor term in falling under the middle term; (2) conclusions that are 'subordinate' to the conclusion, i.e., the conclusion holds for everything that falls under the minor term. Second, if the essential conclusion is universal negative, the same accidental conclusions apply regardless of the figure (first, second, or fourth). Third, if the essential conclusion is particular affirmative, similar accidental conclusions follow except for the subsumption (استتباع) of what is under the minor term under the conclusion, because that would require treating the conclusion as a major premise and something under it as a minor premise, which is not useful here since both are particular. Fourth, if the essential conclusion is particular negative, it does not entail the converse or contrapositive because those are only defined for universal propositions, nor does it entail what falls under it (similar to the reason for particular affirmative). Instead, it only entails a similar judgment for anything that shares with it in relation to the middle term. The author further clarifies that the entailment of the converse or contrapositive is not directly from the syllogism but is a consequent of a consequent (لازم اللازم). Similarly, the entailment of the conclusion for things under the minor term or equal to it is apparent only after the conclusion is known, not from the syllogism itself. This passage demonstrates the logician's concern for precision in distinguishing what is essential from what is accidental in reasoning.

Key Terms
نتيجة بالذات
نتيجة بالعرض
عكس مستوي
عكس النقيض
حد أصغر
حد أوسط

Key Takeaway
Understanding the distinction between essential and accidental conclusions is crucial for correctly expanding the logical consequences of a syllogism without overstepping the original proof.

Accidental Conclusions in Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author classifies accidental conclusions from conjunctive syllogisms by quality and quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the accidental conclusions that follow from a conjunctive syllogism based on the quality and quantity of its per se result.

Core Idea
A syllogism's per se result spawns accidental conclusions depending on its form.

Every syllogism that yields a per se result may also produce accidental results. These are not directly derived from the syllogism itself, but follow when additional hidden premises are assumed. In this lesson, we trace how the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of the original conclusion determine which accidental conclusions arise — from converse and contrapositive to implications under the minor term or equal to it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the per se result's form
Determine whether the original conclusion of the conjunctive syllogism is universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), or particular negative (O). This classification drives all that follows.
The author assumes the syllogism is valid.
2
For universal affirmative: list the accidental kinds
A universal affirmative result (e.g., 'Every B is C') yields two types of accidental conclusions: 'result with result' (like the converse 'Some C is B', contrapositive 'Every non-C is non-B', and implications for terms sharing the minor term in the middle term) and 'result under result' (implications for terms under the minor term, e.g., 'Every D under B is C').
These are the most abundant accidental conclusions.
3
Apply the pattern to other quantities/qualities
For universal negative, similar types apply regardless of figure. For particular affirmative, 'result under result' is excluded because it would use the particular as a major premise with a particular minor, which is not useful. For particular negative, neither converse nor contrapositive exist, nor implications under the minor term; only a similar judgment for terms sharing the relation to the middle term remains.
The author explains the exceptions for I and O.
4
Understand the hidden premises
Accidental implications like those under the minor term are not direct results of the original syllogism. They require a deleted minor premise (e.g., 'Every D is B') to combine with the original conclusion as major. Similarly, implications for terms equal to the minor term need a deleted major premise. Recognizing these hidden components prevents confusion.
The author warns against mistaking these derived syllogisms for the original.

Source Text

نقول: كل قياس أنتج نتيجة بالذات فقد ينتج نتائج أخرى بالعرض، ويختلف ذلك بحسب اختلاف القياس من الاقتراني والاستثنائي ونتيجته، فإن كان القياس اقترانياً فنتيجته إما كليّة موجبة أو سالبة، وإما جزئية موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت نتيجة الاقتران بالذات كلية موجبة فما يلزمه بالعرض ما هو نتيجة مع نتيجة، ومنه ما هو نتيجة تحت نتيجة، فالأول كالعكس المستوي للنتيجة، وعكس النقيض كما عرف، وكما في لزوم النتيجة في كل ما هو مشارك للحد الأصغر في دخوله تحت الحد الأوسط، والثاني فكلزومها في كل ما هو داخل تحت الحد الأصغر، وعلى هذا تكون النتائج العرضية، إن كانت نتيجة الاقتران كليّة سالبة، من غير مبالاة بكون الاقتران من الشكل الأول أو الثاني، أو الرابع، وكذا إن كانت جزئية موجبة ما عدا استتباع ما تحت الحد الأصغر للنتيجة؛ لأن استتباعه في الحقيقة ليس يمكن إلا مع فرض النتيجة كبرى وفرض ما تحتها بالنسبة إليها صغرى وذلك غير مفيد هاهنا؛ لكونها جزئية وما تحتها جزئي. وأما إن كانت النتيجة جزئية سالبة، فهي لا تستتبع العكس ولا عكس النقيض؛ إذ ليس هو لها، ولا ما هو داخل تحتها، لما حققناه في الجزئية الموجبة، بل إنما تستتبع مثل حكمها في ما هو مشارك لها في النسبة إلى الحد الأوسط، فلزوم العكس وعكس النقيض ليس عن نفس القياس، وإنما هو لازم اللازم ولزوم النتيجة في ما هو داخل تحت الحد الأصغر، وفي ما هو مساءٍ له في نسبته إلى الحد الأوسط، وإن كان لزومه ظاهراً عند ظهور النتيجة، فليس عن نفس القياس المذكور بالذات، ولا عن نفس النتيجة بالذات أيضاً، بل الأول إنما يلزم بالذات عن النتيجة، إذا جعلت كبرى، وعن مقدمةٍ صغرى محذوفة موضوعها، كذا الداخل تحت الحد الأصغر، ومحمولها الحد الأصغر ، وذلك كما لو كانت نتيجة الاقتران مثلاً (كل ب" ج") وما هو داخل تحت "ب" "د") فإنه إذا قيل (كل "د" "ب") فـ (كل "ب" "ج" فيلزم منه (كل) "د" "ج") ، وأما الثاني فإنما لزومه بالذات عن المقدمة الكبرى مقدمة أخرى محذوفة وهي ما موضوعها هو المساوي للحد الأصغر في نسبته إلى الحد الأوسط، ومحمولها محمول الصغرى، وذلك كما لو كانت النتيجة (كل) "ب" "ج") والحد الأوسط أو ما يساوي "ب" في نسبتها إلى "أ" هو "د" فإنه إذا قيل (كل "د" "أ") و(كل "أ" "ج") كان اللازم ( كل "د" "ج") لكنه لما كان أخطار هذه الموضوعات بالذهن واتصالها به معاً ربما ظن أن قياسها هو القياس الأول، وعليك أن تتنبه من الاقتراني على ما في الاستثنائي.
Chapter Seven: On Eliciting Conclusions. We say: Every syllogism that produces a result per se may produce other results per accidens, and that differs according to the difference of the syllogism — whether conjunctive or exceptive — and its result. For if the syllogism is conjunctive, its result is either universal affirmative or negative, or particular affirmative or negative. If the per se result of the conjunctive is universal affirmative, then what follows from it per accidens is either a result along with the result (result with result) or a result under the result. The first is like the simple converse of the result and the contrapositive as known, and like the implication of the result in everything that shares the minor term in its falling under the middle term. The second is like its implication in everything that falls under the minor term. On this basis the accidental results are — if the conjunctive result is universal negative — without regard to whether the conjunctive is of the first, second, or fourth figure. Similarly if it is particular affirmative, except for the entailment of what is under the minor term for the result, because its entailment in reality is only possible with the assumption of the result as major premise and the assumption of what is under it relative to it as minor premise, and that is not useful here because it is particular and what is under it is particular. As for if the result is particular negative, it does not entail the converse nor the contrapositive, because it has none, nor does it entail what is under it, for what we established in the particular affirmative. Rather it only entails a similar judgment regarding what shares with it in relation to the middle term. So the implication of the converse and contrapositive is not from the syllogism itself, but rather it is a necessary concomitant of the concomitant, and the implication of the result in what falls under the minor term and in what is equal to it in its relation to the middle term — even if its implication is apparent when the result appears — is not from that mentioned syllogism per se, nor from the result itself per se either. Rather the first is entailed per se from the result when it is made a major premise, and from a deleted minor premise whose subject is that which falls under the minor term and whose predicate is the minor term. For example, if the result of the conjunctive is (Every B is C) and what falls under B is D, then if it is said (Every D is B) and (Every B is C), then (Every D is C) follows. As for the second, its implication per se is from the major premise — another deleted premise whose subject is that which is equal to the minor term in its relation to the middle term, and whose predicate is the predicate of the minor premise. For example, if the result is (Every B is C) and the middle term or what equals B in its relation to A is D, then if it is said (Every D is A) and (Every A is C), the necessary consequent is (Every D is C). But because bringing these subjects to mind and their connection together, one might think that its syllogism is the first syllogism. And you must deduce from the conjunctive what applies in the exceptive.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the accidental conclusions that arise from conjunctive syllogisms. The author begins by stating the general principle: any syllogism that produces a per se result may also produce other results per accidens. The accidentality depends on the type of syllogism (conjunctive or exceptive) and the nature of the result. The focus here is on conjunctive syllogisms, where the result can be one of four categorical forms: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. For a universal affirmative result, the accidental results are of two kinds: 'result with result' and 'result under result.' The first includes the simple converse and contrapositive of the result, as well as the implication of the result for any term that shares the minor term in falling under the middle term. The second covers implications for terms that fall under the minor term. Next, the author explains how the same principle applies when the result is universal negative—the accidental results are similar regardless of the figure (first, second, or fourth). For a particular affirmative result, the same types apply except that the entailment of what falls under the minor term is excluded because that would require assuming the particular result as a major premise with a particular minor premise, which is not useful. For a particular negative result, neither the converse nor the contrapositive follow, nor does implication for terms under the minor term; only a similar judgment for terms sharing the relation to the middle term is entailed. The author then clarifies the logical sources: the converse and contrapositive are not entailed directly by the original syllogism but are 'necessary concomitants of a concomitant.' The implication for terms under the minor term arises when the result is used as a major premise with a suppressed minor premise whose subject is the term under the minor term and whose predicate is the minor term—an example with variables illustrates this. Similarly, implication for terms equal to the minor term in relation to the middle term requires a suppressed major premise. The author warns that one might mistakenly think these derived syllogisms are the original one. Finally, the reader is urged to analogize from the conjunctive to the exceptive syllogism.

A) The simple converse: 'Some haram is an intoxicant'.
B) The contrapositive: 'What is not haram is not an intoxicant'.
C) The implication for a term under the minor term: e.g., 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' then 'Every nabidh is haram'.
D) The implication for a term sharing the middle term: e.g., 'Every alcohol is an intoxicant' and 'Every intoxicant is haram'.

Key Takeaway
The accidental conclusions from a conjunctive syllogism depend critically on the quality and quantity of the per se result; universal affirmative yields the most, while particular negative yields only a limited implication for co-equal terms.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Two Types of Enthymemes in Categorical Syllogisms
Author explains two forms of missing-premise arguments in conjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the two types of missing-premise enthymemes in categorical syllogisms and reconstruct the omitted premise in each type.

Core Idea
An enthymeme is a syllogism with a missing premise, but there are two distinct patterns of omission.

In the Islamic logical tradition, syllogisms are often given with a missing premise. This passage from Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq explains two distinct types of such enthymemes in conjunctive (categorical) syllogisms. Understanding these patterns is crucial for properly evaluating arguments in kalam and fiqh where premises are sometimes implied. Let us walk through the author's explanation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first type of enthymeme
The author describes the first type: the major premise is given, and the minor premise is omitted. The subject of the missing minor premise is what is subsumed under the minor term of the conclusion, and its predicate is the minor term itself.
Example: from 'Every B is C' and D is under B, we reconstruct 'Every D is B' as the missing premise.
2
Reconstruct the first type with notation
Take the conclusion 'Every B is C' and an additional term D that is under B. The omitted minor premise is 'Every D is B'. Then the full syllogism becomes: 'Every D is B', 'Every B is C', therefore 'Every D is C'.
The middle term here is B, connecting D and C.
3
Identify the second type of enthymeme
The second type involves a missing premise that is entailed by the major premise. The subject of the omitted premise equals the minor term in its relation to the middle term, and its predicate is the predicate of the minor premise.
Example: conclusion 'Every B is C', and D is equal to B in relation to A. The omitted premise is 'Every D is A', then with 'Every A is C' we deduce 'Every D is C'.
4
Reconstruct the second type with notation
Given conclusion 'Every B is C' and that D is equal to B in relation to A, the missing premise is 'Every D is A'. Combined with the major premise 'Every A is C', we get conclusion 'Every D is C'.
Here the middle term is A, connecting D and C.
5
Distinguish the two types carefully
The author warns that one might mistakenly think the second type is the same as the first, because both involve connecting a term D to C via a middle term. However, in the first type the missing premise connects D directly to B (the minor term), while in the second type the missing premise connects D to a new term A that equals B in relation to the middle term.
This distinction is important for correctly evaluating whether a given syllogism is valid and which premise is truly omitted.

الفن الثامن في اعتبار أمور نافعة الاعتبار في صور الحجج وعن 223 إذا جعلت كبرى، وعن مقدمةٍ صغرى محذوفة موضوعها، كذا الداخل تحت الحد الأصغر، ومحمولها الحد الأصغر ، وذلك كما لو كانت نتيجة الاقتران مثلاً (كل ب" "ج") وما هو داخل تحت "ب" "د") فإنه إذا قيل (كل "د" "ب") فـ (كل "ب" "ج" فيلزم منه (كل) "د" "ج") ، وأما الثاني فإنما لزومه بالذات عن المقدمة الكبرى مقدمة أخرى محذوفة وهي ما موضوعها هو المساوي للحد الأصغر في نسبته إلى الحد الأوسط، ومحمولها محمول الصغرى، وذلك كما لو كانت النتيجة (كل) "ب" "ج") والحد الأوسط أو ما يساوي "ب" في نسبتها إلى "أ" هو "د" فإنه إذا قيل (كل "د" "أ") و(كل "أ" "ج") كان اللازم ( كل "د" "ج") لكنه لما كان أخطار هذه الموضوعات بالذهن واتصالها به معاً ربما ظن أن قياسها هو القياس الأول، وعليك أن تتنبه من الاقتراني على ما في الاستثنائي.
The eighth art concerning the consideration of beneficial matters in the forms of arguments. And from 223: If you make a major premise, and from a minor premise whose subject is omitted, likewise what is subsumed under the minor term, and its predicate is the minor term. That is as if the conclusion of the conjunctive is for example 'Every B is C' and what is subsumed under 'B' is 'D'. Then if it is said 'Every D is B' and 'Every B is C', it follows 'Every D is C'. As for the second, its entailment essentially from the major premise [to] another omitted premise, which is one whose subject is equal to the minor term in its relation to the middle term, and its predicate is the predicate of the minor premise. That is as if the conclusion is 'Every B is C' and the middle term or what equals B in its relation to A is 'D', then if it is said 'Every D is A' and 'Every A is C', the necessary is 'Every D is C'. But because the bringing to mind of these subjects and their connection together perhaps leads one to think that its syllogism is the first syllogism, you must be aware from the conjunctive [syllogism] to what is in the conditional.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage explains two types of enthymemes (missing-premise arguments) in conjunctive (categorical) syllogisms. The author first describes the first type: when the major premise is given and the minor premise is omitted. The omitted minor premise has a subject that is subsumed under the minor term of the conclusion, and its predicate is that minor term. For example, if the conclusion is 'Every B is C' and D is under B, then from 'Every D is B' and 'Every B is C' we get 'Every D is C'. The second type involves a missing premise that is entailed by the major premise itself. Here, the subject of the omitted premise equals the minor term in its relation to the middle term, and its predicate is the predicate of the minor premise. Example: conclusion 'Every B is C', middle term D equal to B in relation to A, then from 'Every D is A' and 'Every A is C' we get 'Every D is C'. The author warns that one might mistakenly think the second type is the same as the first, but they are distinct. The lesson is to carefully distinguish between conjunctive and conditional syllogisms when dealing with such enthymemes.

An enthymeme in a categorical syllogism can omit either the minor premise (type 1) or a premise paralleling the minor premise via an equal term (type 2); correctly identifying the type is essential for proper logical analysis.

The Syllogism

Two Causes of Ignorance and the Premise Connection
Author identifies two causes of ignorance and defends against infinite regress objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ignorance due to a missing premise and ignorance due to forgetfulness of the connection between premises, and explain how the author avoids infinite regress by treating the connection as an attribute.

Core Idea
The connection between premises is an attribute, not a third premise, saving syllogism from infinite regress.

In logical proofs, even when both premises are known, we may still fail to draw the conclusion. This passage identifies two distinct causes: ignorance of the minor premise itself, or forgetfulness of the connection between the premises. An objection then threatens the very structure of syllogism: if the connection is an extra element, it would require infinite premises. The author's ingenious reply preserves the sufficiency of two premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two causes of ignorance
Read the first part of the passage where the author states that ignorance of a particular matter can arise either from ignorance of the minor premise (e.g., not knowing that the object in Zayd's hand is equally divisible) or from forgetfulness of the connection between the minor and major premises (e.g., knowing a mule is barren but forgetting the link to the apparent pregnancy).
The examples illustrate each cause clearly.
2
Analyze the Zayd's hand example
We know all equally divisible things are even. But we don't know that the object in Zayd's hand is equally divisible. So ignorance stems from lacking the minor premise. The major premise is known.
The object is actually divisible in itself, but we are ignorant of that fact.
3
Analyze the mule example
We know the mule is a mule (minor) and every mule is barren (major). Yet we believe it is pregnant because we fail to connect the two premises — we forgot the link that would produce the conclusion 'the mule is not pregnant.'
Both premises are present in the mind, but the composition is missing.
4
Understand the objection against the connection
The objector argues: If the connection between premises is something additional to the premises themselves, then knowing that connection would itself be a third premise, leading to an infinite regress of required premises. Conversely, if it is not additional, then simply knowing the two premises should automatically yield the conclusion, but we see cases (like the mule) where it does not.
The objection threatens the foundational idea that only two premises are needed.
5
Examine the author's response
The author concedes that the connection is additional, but denies that it requires another connection. He argues that the connection is an attribute (ṣifa) of the premises. Attributes do not need further attributes to link them to their subject; they subsist directly. Thus, no infinite regress arises.
This preserves the two-premise structure while accounting for the need to actively consider the connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثامن في اعتبار أمور نافعة الاعتبار في صور الحُجَج
225
نعم، قد يتفق أن يكون الجهل بالأمر الجزئي بالفعل للجهل بالمقدمة الصغرى، وقد يكون للغفلة عن الارتباط الواقع بينها وبين الكبرى، وإن خطرا بالبال معاً، أما الأول فكما في جهلنا بزوجية ما في يد زيد بالفعل، لجهلنا بانقسامه بمتساويين، وإن كنا نعلم أن كل منقسم بمتساويين زوج وليس يمكن أن يقال إنه غير معلوم بالقوة، وداخل تحت المعلوم الكلي ظناً أن المعلوم الكلي هو، وأن كل منقسم بمتساويين علمناه فهو زوج، وما في يد زيد غير معلوم أنه منقسم بمتساويين، فإن عاقلاً لا يرتاب في العلم بأن كل ما هو منقسم بمتساويين في نفسه فهو زوج، وإن كان يجهل كونه منقسماً، وما في يد زيد منقسم بمتساويين في نفسه لا محالة، فيكون معلوماً لنا أنه زوج، وأن جهلنا انقسامه بالفعل، وأما الثاني فكما في اعتقادنا أن هذه البغلة المنتفخة البطن حُبلى، مع علمنا أنها ،بغلة، وأن كل بغلة عقيم، وليس ذلك إلا للغفلة عن الارتباط الواقع بين المقدمتين، والترتيب الذي من شأنه أن ينتج ، إذ العلم بالمقدمتين وخطورهما في البال اتفق على وجه التفريق من غير مراعاة لما بينها من التأليف المخصوص، وخطورهما في البال غير مفيد.
فإن قيل: المفهوم من الارتباط الواقع بين المقدمتين، وكيفية اندراج أحدهما تحت الأخرى، إما أن تزيد على نفس المقدمتين أو لا تزيد عليهما، فإن زاد عليهما فالعلم به علم بمقدمة ثالثة لا بُدّية في الإنتاج، وذلك يستدعي مقدمات لا تتناهى ويلزم منه توقف النتيجة على ما لا يتناهى من المقدمات وهو محال كيف وفيه إبطال ما قررتموه سابقاً، من أن المطلوب لا يفتقر إلى أكثر من المقدمتين، وإن لم یکن ذلك زائداً على المقدمتين فيلزم من العلم بالمقدمتين العلم بلزوم النتيجة؛ ضرورة أن ما توقف عليه العلم بلزوم النتيجة عن المقدمتين، لا يزيد على
المقدمتين.
قلنا: بل ارتباط إحدى المقدمتين بالأخرى، وكيفية اندراج الصغرى تحت الكبرى، زائد على المقدمتين، ولكن لا نسلم أن ذلك يستدعي ارتباطاً آخر لهذا الارتباط بالمقدمتين، فإن ارتباط إحدى المقدمتين بالأخرى صفة للمقدمتين والصفة لا تستدعي في شؤونها لموصوفها صفة أخرى، وإلا لتسلسل الحال، وامتنع
Eighth Art: On considering useful matters in the forms of proofs. 225. Yes, it may happen that ignorance of a particular matter actually is due to ignorance of the minor premise, and it may be due to forgetfulness of the connection that exists between it and the major premise, even if both occur to the mind together. As for the first: as in our actual ignorance of the evenness of what is in Zayd's hand, due to our ignorance of its division into equal parts, even though we know that everything divided into equal parts is even, and it cannot be said that it is not known potentially and falls under universal knowledge, supposing that the universal knowledge is that everything divided into equal parts that we know is even, and what is in Zayd's hand is not known to be divided into equal parts. For a rational person does not doubt the knowledge that everything that is actually divided into equal parts in itself is even, even if he is ignorant of its being divided, and what is in Zayd's hand is inevitably divided into equal parts in itself, so it is known to us that it is even, and our ignorance is of its actual division. As for the second: as in our belief that this swollen-bellied mule is pregnant, despite our knowledge that it is a mule and that every mule is barren. That is only due to forgetfulness of the connection existing between the two premises and the arrangement that should produce the conclusion, because knowledge of the two premises and their occurrence in the mind happened in a separated manner without regard to the specific composition between them, and their occurrence in the mind is not beneficial. If it is said: The understood from the connection existing between the two premises and the manner of subsumption of one under the other is either additional to the two premises themselves or not additional. If it is additional, then knowledge of it is knowledge of a third premise necessary for production, and that requires infinite premises, and it follows that the conclusion depends on infinite premises, which is impossible. Moreover, it invalidates what you previously established that the sought conclusion needs no more than two premises. If it is not additional to the two premises, then knowledge of the two premises entails knowledge of the necessity of the conclusion, necessarily because what knowledge of the conclusion's necessity from the premises depends on is not additional to the two premises. We say: Rather, the connection of one premise to the other and the manner of subsumption of the minor under the major is additional to the two premises, but we do not concede that this requires another connection of this connection to the premises. For the connection of one premise to the other is an attribute of the premises, and an attribute, in its affairs, does not require for its subject another attribute; otherwise, infinite regress would occur, and it would be impossible.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange that first categorizes two causes of ignorance in particular matters, then defends a key logical point against an objection. The author explains that ignorance may arise either (1) from ignorance of the minor premise itself (e.g., not knowing that an object is divided equally), or (2) from forgetfulness of the connection between the minor and major premises, even when both are known separately (e.g., knowing that the mule is a mule and that every mule is barren, yet believing it is pregnant because the connection between the two premises is not actively considered). The objection then raises a critical problem: if the connection between premises is an additional element beyond the two premises, then knowing it would require a third premise, leading to infinite regress. Alternatively, if it is not additional, then simply knowing the premises would automatically yield the conclusion. The author's response is precise: the connection is indeed additional, but it is an attribute (ṣifa) of the premises themselves. Attributes do not require further attributes to link them to their subjects; the connection subsists in the premises without needing an infinite chain. Thus, the regress is avoided, and the sufficiency of two premises for producing a conclusion is preserved.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Why Knowing Both Premises Is Not Enough
Author explains causes of ignorance of conclusions and defends the necessity of awareness of premise connection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between logical entailment (in re) and the knower's awareness of the premise connection, and explain why the latter is necessary for acquiring new knowledge from a syllogism.

Core Idea
The conclusion follows from the premises, but only if you perceive the connection.

You know both premises of a valid syllogism, yet the conclusion does not occur to you. How is that possible? The author of our passage distinguishes between logical necessity in reality and the psychological act of drawing the inference. He defends the view that the connection between premises is a real property requiring awareness, not an extra premise. This resolves the Socratic paradox about syllogistic knowledge: we seek what is known potentially.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two causes of ignorance
The author says ignorance of a particular conclusion can arise from ignorance of the minor premise (the particular's subsumption) or from forgetfulness (ghafla) of the logical connection between the minor and major premises. Note the two examples: the evenness of Zayd's number (ignorance of minor) and the mule pregnancy (forgetfulness of connection).
Focus on the second cause — it is the subject of the rest of the passage.
2
Understand the objector's infinite regress argument
The objector claims: if the connection between premises is something additional to the premises, then knowledge of that connection would be a third premise, requiring another connection, leading to infinite regress. If it is not additional, then knowledge of the premises alone should suffice to know the conclusion.
This is a standard objection against any intermediary between premises and conclusion.
3
Follow the author's response: connection as a property
The author replies that the connection is indeed additional but not as a premise; it is a property (sifa) of the two premises. A property does not require another property linking it to its subject — that would also lead to infinite regress. So the connection is known directly when one attends to the premises together.
This avoids the need for an infinite chain of premises.
4
Examine the three reconciliations with the two-premise principle
The author gives three responses to show no contradiction: (1) the principle of needing only two premises refers to logical entailment in re, not to the knower's awareness; (2) the two premises are those appropriate to the conclusion, and the connection is not appropriate (i.e., it is not a proper premise); (3) the principle holds when one is attentive to the connection — if heedless, the entailment is not realized in the knower.
These differentiate between objective logical structure and subjective knowledge acquisition.
5
Apply to the Socratic paradox
The paradox says: if the conclusion is known, seeking it is futile; if unknown, we cannot recognize it when found. The author resolves it: the conclusion is known potentially and universally (with a middle term as a sign), but actually unknown due to ignorance of the sign or heedlessness of the composition. Once we attend to the connection, we recognize the conclusion.
The example of the escaped slave with a sign illustrates how we recognize the unknown via a known mark.

Source Text

الفن الثامن في اعتبار أمور نافعة الاعتبار في صور الحجج
نعم، قد يتفق أن يكون الجهل بالأمر الجزئي بالفعل للجهل بالمقدمة الصغرى، وقد يكون للغفلة عن الارتباط الواقع بينها وبين الكبرى، وإن خطرا بالبال معاً، أما الأول فكما في جهلنا بزوجية ما في يد زيد بالفعل، لجهلنا بانقسامه بمتساويين، وإن كنا نعلم أن كل منقسم بمتساويين زوج وليس يمكن أن يقال إنه غير معلوم بالقوة، وداخل تحت المعلوم الكلي ظناً أن المعلوم الكلي هو، وأن كل منقسم بمتساويين علمناه فهو زوج، وما في يد زيد غير معلوم أنه منقسم بمتساويين، فإن عاقلاً لا يرتاب في العلم بأن كل ما هو منقسم بمتساويين في نفسه فهو زوج، وإن كان يجهل كونه منقسماً، وما في يد زيد منقسم بمتساويين في نفسه لا محالة، فيكون معلوماً لنا أنه زوج، وأن جهلنا انقسامه بالفعل، وأما الثاني فكما في اعتقادنا أن هذه البغلة المنتفخة البطن حُبلى، مع علمنا أنها بغلة، وأن كل بغلة عقيم، وليس ذلك إلا للغفلة عن الارتباط الواقع بين المقدمتين، والترتيب الذي من شأنه أن ينتج، إذ العلم بالمقدمتين وخطورهما في البال اتفق على وجه التفريق من غير مراعاة لما بينها من التأليف المخصوص، وخطورهما في البال غير مفيد.
فإن قيل: المفهوم من الارتباط الواقع بين المقدمتين، وكيفية اندراج أحدهما تحت الأخرى، إما أن تزيد على نفس المقدمتين أو لا تزيد عليهما، فإن زاد عليهما فالعلم به علم بمقدمة ثالثة لا بُدّية في الإنتاج، وذلك يستدعي مقدمات لا تتناهى ويلزم منه توقف النتيجة على ما لا يتناهى من المقدمات وهو محال كيف وفيه إبطال ما قررتموه سابقاً، من أن المطلوب لا يفتقر إلى أكثر من المقدمتين، وإن لم يكن ذلك زائداً على المقدمتين فيلزم من العلم بالمقدمتين العلم بلزوم النتيجة؛ ضرورة أن ما توقف عليه العلم بلزوم النتيجة عن المقدمتين، لا يزيد على المقدمتين.
قلنا: بل ارتباط إحدى المقدمتين بالأخرى، وكيفية اندراج الصغرى تحت الكبرى، زائد على المقدمتين، ولكن لا نسلم أن ذلك يستدعي ارتباطاً آخر لهذا الارتباط بالمقدمتين، فإن ارتباط إحدى المقدمتين بالأخرى صفة للمقدمتين والصفة لا تستدعي في شؤونها لموصوفها صفة أخرى، وإلا لتسلسل الحال، وامتنع قيام الصفات بموضوعاتها وهو محال بل ارتباطها بالمقدمتين بنفسه لا بارتباط زائد عليه وقولهم أن هذا يناقض ما قررتموه من كون المطلوب لا يفتقر إلى أكثر من مقدمتين فمندفع من وجوه
الأول: أن المراد من قولنا أن المطلوب لا يفتقر إلى أكثر من مقدمتين - أي في لزومه في نفس الأمر - لا في نظر الناظر، والمطلوب في ما ذكرناه هاهنا من الأمثلة، فلازم في نفس الأمر عن مجرد المقدمة الصغرى والكبرى، غير أن اشتراط الشعور بارتباط إحدى المقدمتين بالأخرى، إنما كان بالنسبة إلى علم الناظر، فلا تناقض
الثاني: أن المراد بالمقدمتين اللتين لا يفتقر المطلوب إلى أكثر منهما، إنما هي المقدمات المناسبة للمطلوب على ما قدمناه والارتباط بين المقدمتين وإن كان زائداً على المقدمتين غير مناسب للمطلوب، ولا لأحد جزأيه، فلا تناقض
الثالث: أنه أمكن أن يقال المراد به المقدمتان اللتان إذا تنبه على الارتباط الواقع بينهما لزم عنهما المطلوب، فعدم لزوم المطلوب عن المقدمتين اللتين غفل عن الارتباط الواقع بينهما لا يوجب التناقض، وعلى هذا فقد انحل الشك الوارد على سقراط في زعمه إفادة القياس للمطلوب، وهو إن قيل المطلوب به إما معلوم وإما مجهول، فإن كان معلوماً فلا معنى لطلبه؛ إذ هو كمن يفتش على آبق هو واجد له - أي عينه وفي قبضته، وإن كان مجهولاً فلا فائدة في طلبه؛ إذ لا نعرفه عند الظفر به وليس هو إلا كمن يفتش على آبق لا يعلم عينه، فإنَّا كما قال أفلاطن الحكيم - ما هذا معناه - إنَّا لا نطلب ما هو معلوم من كل وجه، ولا ما هو مجهول من كل وجه حتى يلزم ما قيل، بل يطلب ما هو معلوم لنا بالقوة على نحو كلي، وعليه علامة هي كالحد الأوسط بين جزأي المطلوب، وجهول بالفعل إما للجهل بالعلامة، أو للغفلة عن التأليف والارتباط كما سبق فإذا وجدناه على العلامة المعلومة لنا كنا متنبهين للارتباط، وما ذلك البحث عن الآبق إذا كانت فيه علامة تدل عليه وإنما جهلت أنيته، فإنه إذا رأى على تلك العلامة علم أنه
The eighth section on considering beneficial matters in the forms of arguments. Yes, it may happen that ignorance of a particular matter in actuality is due to ignorance of the minor premise, and may be due to forgetfulness of the connection between it and the major, even if both occur to the mind simultaneously. As for the first, like our ignorance of the evenness of what is in Zayd's hand in actuality, due to our ignorance of its divisibility into two equal parts, even though we know that everything divisible into two equal parts is even. It cannot be said that it is unknown potentially and is included under the universal knowledge, thinking that the universal knowledge is that; and that every divisible into two equal parts that we know about is even, and what is in Zayd's hand is not known to be divisible into two equal parts. For a rational person does not doubt the knowledge that everything that is divisible into two equal parts in itself is even, even if he is ignorant of its being divisible, and what is in Zayd's hand is divisible into two equal parts in itself without doubt, so it is known to us that it is even, and that our ignorance is of its actual divisibility. As for the second, like our belief that this mule with a swollen belly is pregnant, while we know that it is a mule, and that every mule is barren. That is only due to forgetfulness of the connection between the two premises and the arrangement that would produce the conclusion, because knowledge of the two premises and their occurrence in the mind happened in a disjunctive manner without regard to the specific composition between them, and their occurrence in the mind is not beneficial. If it is said: The understood from the connection between the two premises and the manner of subsumption of one under the other either adds to the two premises themselves or does not add. If it adds, then knowledge of it is knowledge of a third premise necessary for production, and that requires infinite premises, and necessitates the conclusion's dependence on infinite premises, which is impossible; moreover, it invalidates what you previously established, that the desired conclusion does not need more than two premises. If it does not add, then from knowledge of the two premises follows knowledge of the necessity of the conclusion, necessarily, because that on which depends knowledge of the necessity of the conclusion from the two premises does not exceed the two premises. We say: Rather, the connection of one premise with the other and the manner of subsumption of the minor under the major is additional to the two premises, but we do not concede that that requires another connection linking this connection to the two premises. For the connection of one premise with the other is a property of the two premises, and a property, in its affairs, does not require for its subject another property; otherwise there would be an infinite regress of states, and the subsistence of properties in their subjects would be impossible—that is absurd. Rather, their connection to the two premises is by itself, not by an additional connection. And their statement that this contradicts what you established—that the desired conclusion does not need more than two premises—is rebutted from several aspects. First: That what is meant by our statement that the desired conclusion does not need more than two premises is in its logical necessity in re, not in the view of the viewer. The desired conclusion in the examples we have mentioned is necessary in re from just the minor and major premises; however, the condition of awareness of the connection of one premise with the other is only with respect to the viewer's knowledge, so there is no contradiction. Second: That what is meant by the two premises that the desired conclusion does not need more than them are the premises appropriate to the conclusion, as we have presented. And the connection between the two premises, even if additional to them, is not appropriate to the conclusion nor to either of its parts, so there is no contradiction. Third: That it is possible to say that what is meant is the two premises that, when one is attentive to the connection between them, the desired conclusion follows from them. So the non-conclusion of the desired conclusion from the two premises from which one was heedless of the connection does not necessitate contradiction. And thus the doubt raised against Socrates in his claim that syllogism yields the desired conclusion is resolved. It is said: The desired conclusion is either known or unknown. If it is known, then seeking it is meaningless, like someone searching for an escaped slave who already has him—i.e., his very self in his grasp. If it is unknown, then seeking it is useless, because we do not recognize him when we find him, and it is like someone searching for an escaped slave whose identity he does not know. But we, as the wise Plato said—the meaning of this—do not seek what is known in every respect, nor what is unknown in every respect, such that what was said would follow. Rather, what is sought is known to us potentially in a universal manner, and upon it is a sign that is like the middle term between the two parts of the desired conclusion, and it is actually unknown due either to ignorance of the sign or to heedlessness of the composition and connection as mentioned. So when we find it by the known sign, we become attentive to the connection. And that is like the search for the escaped slave when there is a sign indicating him, but his essence was unknown. For when he sees that sign, he knows that...
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about how syllogistic arguments produce new knowledge. The author begins by distinguishing two causes of ignorance of a particular conclusion despite knowing the universal premises: either ignorance of the minor premise (e.g., not knowing that Zayd's number is divisible into two equal parts) or forgetfulness of the logical connection between the two premises (e.g., knowing that a mule is barren but not linking that to the mule's swollen belly, wrongly thinking it pregnant). The second cause is the focus. The author then presents an objection: if the connection between premises is additional to the premises themselves, it would require a third premise, leading to infinite regress; if not additional, then knowing the premises should automatically yield the conclusion. The response is that the connection is additional as a property of the premises, but it does not require another connection because properties do not need further properties. Then three responses are given to the charge of contradiction with the earlier principle that only two premises are needed: (1) the principle refers to the logical entailment in re, not to the knower's awareness; (2) the two premises are specifically those appropriate to the conclusion; (3) the principle applies when one is attentive to the connection. Finally, the author resolves the Socratic paradox about syllogistic knowledge: we seek what is known potentially and universally, with a middle term as a sign; ignorance arises either from not knowing the sign or from forgetfulness of the composition. The example of searching for an escaped slave with a defining mark illustrates how we recognize the conclusion via the middle term.

Key Terms

المقدمة الصغرى
المقدمة الكبرى
الحد الأوسط

Checkpoint
In the example of the mule with a swollen belly, why did the person wrongly believe it was pregnant despite knowing that all mules are barren?
A) He was ignorant of the major premise.
B) He was ignorant that the animal was a mule.
C) He forgot the logical connection between the two premises.
D) He did not know that the belly swelling indicates pregnancy.

Key Takeaway
The logical connection between premises is a property that must be grasped subjectively for the syllogism to yield new knowledge; it is not an extra premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: In Usul al-Fiqh, a jurist knows the general rule 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (from hadith) and knows that 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' (based on evidence). Yet he fails to conclude that nabidh is forbidden. Using the author's distinction, explain the possible cause of his failure. Is it ignorance of the minor premise or heedlessness of the connection?
