The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Three Refutations and the Resolution of the Socratic Paradox
Three refutations of the doubt that a syllogism requires more than two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three distinct responses to the objection that a syllogism cannot produce new knowledge, and explain how potential knowledge via the middle term resolves the paradox.

Core Idea
A syllogism's conclusion is logically entailed by the premises alone; subjective awareness of their connection is a separate condition.

Some thinkers have objected that if the conclusion of a syllogism is already known, seeking it is pointless; if unknown, we cannot recognize it. This paradox seems to undermine all theoretical inquiry. The author provides three powerful refutations and resolves the issue by invoking the concept of potential knowledge and the middle term as a sign. This lesson unpacks those arguments and shows how they safeguard the validity of syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection states that the conclusion must be either known or unknown. If known, seeking it is like searching for a runaway slave you already have; if unknown, you cannot identify it when found. Therefore, syllogism cannot yield new knowledge.
This is a classic dilemma against the possibility of learning.
2
First refutation: logical necessity vs. subjective knowledge
The author distinguishes between the objective necessity of the conclusion (it follows from the premises in themselves) and the subjective awareness of the connection. The conclusion is necessary in itself from the two premises alone; the condition of perceiving the connection is only for the knower. Thus no contradiction arises.
The connection (irtibāṭ) between premises is not an additional element but is inherent.
3
Second refutation: premises appropriate to the conclusion
The principle 'the conclusion needs only two premises' refers to premises that are directly appropriate to the conclusion. The connection between premises is not part of the conclusion or its parts, so it does not violate the principle even if it is an 'extra' element.
The connection is not a premise itself.
4
Third refutation: awareness of connection as condition
The intended meaning is that if one becomes aware of the connection, the conclusion follows. Heedlessness of the connection does not mean the conclusion does not follow objectively; it only means the observer does not yet have knowledge. So there is no inconsistency.
This answer directly addresses the psychological aspect of reasoning.
5
Resolution using potential knowledge and the middle term
The author presents Plato's solution: we do not seek what is completely known or completely unknown. We seek what is known potentially in a universal way, with a sign (the middle term) that connects the parts. The sign (e.g., divisibility) is known universally; when we find it in the particular, we recognize the conclusion. This avoids infinite regress or the impossibility of theoretical inquiry.
The middle term (ḥadd awsaṭ) is the key that actualizes potential knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيام الصفات بموضوعاتها وهو محال بل ارتباطها بالمقدمتين بنفسه لا بارتباط زائد عليه وقولهم أن هذا يناقض ما قررتموه من كون المطلوب لا يفتقر إلى أكثر من مقدمتين فمندفع من وجوه الأول: أن المراد من قولنا أن المطلوب لا يفتقر إلى أكثر من مقدمتين) - أي في لزومه في نفس الأمر - لا في نظر الناظر، والمطلوب في ما ذكرناه هاهنا من الأمثلة، فلازم في نفس الأمر عن مجرد المقدمة الصغرى والكبرى، غير أن اشتراط الشعور بارتباط إحدى المقدمتين بالأخرى، إنما كان بالنسبة إلى علم الناظر، فلا تناقض الثاني: أن المراد بالمقدمتين اللتين لا يفتقر المطلوب إلى أكثر منهما، إنما هي المقدمات المناسبة للمطلوب على ما قدمناه والارتباط بين المقدمتين وإن كان زائداً على المقدمتين غير مناسب للمطلوب، ولا لأحد جزأيه، فلا تناقض الثالث: أنه أمكن أن يقال المراد به المقدمتان اللتان إذا تنبه على الارتباط الواقع بينهما لزم عنهما المطلوب، فعدم لزوم المطلوب عن المقدمتين اللتين غفل عن الارتباط الواقع بينهما لا يوجب التناقض، وعلى هذا فقد انحل الشك الوارد على سقراط في زعمه إفادة القياس للمطلوب، وهو إن قيل المطلوب به إما معلوم وإما مجهول، فإن كان معلوماً فلا معنى لطلبه؛ إذ هو كمن يفتش على آبق هو واجد له - أي عينه وفي قبضته ، وإن كان مجهولاً فلا فائدة في طلبه؛ إذ لا نعرفه عند الظفر به وليس هو إلا كمن يفتش على آبق لا يعلم عينه، فإنَّا كما قال أفلاطن الحكيم - ما هذا معناه - إنَّا لا نطلب ما هو معلوم من كل وجه، ولا ما هو مجهول من كل وجه حتى يلزم ما قيل، بل يطلب ما هو معلوم لنا بالقوة على نحو كلي، وعليه علامة هي كالحد الأوسط بين جزأي المطلوب، وجهول بالفعل إما للجهل بالعلامة، أو للغفلة عن التأليف والارتباط كما سبق فإذا وجدناه على العلامة المعلومة لنا كنا متنبهين للارتباط، وما ذلك البحث عن الآبق إذا كانت فيه علامة تدل عليه وإنما جهلت أنيته، فإنه إذا رأى على تلك العلامة علم أنه المطلوب، وذلك كما إذا علمنا أن كل ما هو منقسم بمتساويين زوج، فإنا إذا بحثنا عما في يد زيد وجدنا له الحد الأوسط الذي هو علامة عليه، وهو الانقسام بمتساويين، وكنا مع ذلك متنبهين للارتباط بين المقدمتين علمنا أنه زوج وليس لا يكون الطلب إلا لما كان معلوماً وقد جهل كما ظن قوم، وإلا للزم التسلسل في كل مطلوب نظري، أو أن تكون جميع المطلوبات غير نظرية وهو محال.
The attribution of attributes to their subjects is impossible; rather, the connection of the premises to each other is by themselves, not by an additional connection. And their saying that this contradicts what you established—that the conclusion does not need more than two premises—is repelled from several aspects. First: what is meant by our saying 'the conclusion does not need more than two premises' is with respect to its necessity in itself, not in the view of the observer. The conclusion in the examples we mentioned here is necessary in itself from merely the minor and major premises; however, the condition of perceiving the connection of one premise to the other is only with respect to the knowledge of the observer, so there is no contradiction. Second: what is meant by the two premises that the conclusion does not need more than them are the premises appropriate to the conclusion as we have stated. The connection between the premises, even if it is additional to the two premises, is not appropriate to the conclusion nor to either of its parts, so there is no contradiction. Third: it is possible to say that what is meant are the two premises such that when one becomes aware of the connection existing between them, the conclusion follows from them. So the non-necessity of the conclusion from the two premises when one is heedless of the connection between them does not necessitate contradiction. And on this basis, the doubt raised against Socrates in his claim that the syllogism yields the conclusion is resolved. It is said: the sought conclusion is either known or unknown. If it is known, then seeking it is meaningless, like someone searching for a runaway slave he already has—i.e., his very person in his grasp. And if it is unknown, then seeking it is futile, for we do not recognize it when we come upon it; it is only like someone searching for a runaway slave whose person he does not know. But we—as the wise Plato said, in meaning—do not seek what is known in every respect, nor what is unknown in every respect, so that what was said would follow. Rather, we seek what is known to us potentially in a universal manner, and upon it there is a sign that is like the middle term between the two parts of the conclusion, and it is actually unknown either due to ignorance of the sign or due to heedlessness of the composition and connection, as previously. So when we find it with the sign known to us, we become aware of the connection. And that is like the search for the runaway slave: if there is a sign indicating him, and only his essence is unknown, then when one sees that sign, one knows he is the sought one. And that is like when we know that everything divisible into two equal parts is even. If we search for what is in Zayd's hand, we find for it the middle term that is the sign, namely divisibility into two equal parts, and we are, with that, aware of the connection between the two premises, we know it is even. Indeed, seeking is only for what is known yet unknown, as some people thought. Otherwise, an infinite regress would follow in every theoretical inquiry, or all sought things would be non-theoretical, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection against the standard theory of syllogism. The objection is that if the conclusion follows from two premises, it cannot be new knowledge, because either it was already known (so seeking it is pointless) or it was unknown (so we cannot recognize it when found). The author gives three distinct answers. First, he distinguishes between logical necessity in itself and the actual knowledge of the observer: the conclusion is logically implied by the two premises alone, but the observer must be aware of the connection between them. This awareness is a subjective condition, not an objective addition. Second, he clarifies that the two premises meant are those directly appropriate to the conclusion; the connection between them is not part of the conclusion or its parts, so even if it is an extra element, it does not violate the principle. Third, he reformulates: the two premises are such that if one becomes aware of their connection, the conclusion follows; heedlessness does not entail contradiction. This third answer leads to a resolution of the Socratic paradox. The author explains, following Plato, that we do not seek what is completely known or completely unknown. Rather, we seek what is known potentially and universally, with a sign (the middle term) that connects the parts. The example of the runaway slave illustrates this: we know a sign (like a mark) but not the individual's identity. When we find someone with that sign, we recognize him. Similarly, in the syllogism, we know the universal rule (e.g., everything divisible into two equal parts is even) and we discover via the middle term (divisibility) that a particular item is even. The author concludes that if we did not have this potential knowledge, an infinite regress would occur (each theoretical inquiry would require prior knowledge ad infinitum) or all inquiry would be non-theoretical—both impossible.

حد أوسط

Key Takeaway
The syllogism's conclusion is new knowledge actualized from potential knowledge via the middle term, and the three refutations show that subjective awareness does not affect objective logical necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Deriving Converse of Premises from Converse of Conclusion (Universal Affirmative)
Author derives converses of premises from converse of conclusion for universal affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the converse of each premise in a universal affirmative first-figure syllogism using the converse of the conclusion.

Core Idea
The converse of a conclusion unlocks the converses of its premises.

This passage from al-Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq teaches a powerful method: given a valid universal affirmative syllogism and its converted conclusion, you can recover the converted premises. This technique is essential for working with syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Note the minor premise (كل ب أ), major premise (كل أ ج), and conclusion (كل ب ج). This is a valid first-figure syllogism.
The terms (ب, أ, ج) are placeholders for any Islamic concepts.
2
Write the converse of the conclusion
Swap subject and predicate while preserving quality and truth: كل ج ب.
Universal affirmative converts simply.
3
Derive the converse of the minor premise
Combine the major premise (كل أ ج) with the converse of the conclusion (كل ج ب) in a new syllogism: كل أ ج, كل ج ب ⇒ كل أ ب. This is the converse of the minor premise.
The new syllogism is also first figure, valid.
4
Derive the converse of the major premise
Combine the minor premise (كل ب أ) with the converse of the conclusion (كل ج ب): كل ج ب, كل ب أ ⇒ كل ج أ. This is the converse of the major premise.
This confirms the symmetry of the method.

الفصل التاسع في تحقيق ما يعرض من عكس النتائج والمقدمات والمتلازمات والمتقابلات وغير ذلك من العوارض بسبب أحوال الحدود فنقول: أما ما يعرض من عكس النتائج، فإن المطلوب إذا كان كلياً موجباً، كقولنا : (كل "ب" "ج") وانعكس كنفسه (كل "ج" "ب") وكان قياسه (كل "ب" "أ") و(كل "أ" "ج") لزم عكس كل واحدة من مقدمتيه، أما الصغرى فلأنك إذا قرنت عكس النتيجة بالكبرى فقلت (كل "أ" "ج") و(كل "ج" "ب") أنتج عكس الصغرى، وهو (كل "أ" "ب")، وأما الكبرى فلأنك إذا قرنت عكس النتيجة بالصغرى فقلت (كل "ج" "ب") و(كل "ب" "أ") أنتج عكس الكبرى، وهو (كل "ج" "أ")، وإن كان كلياً سالباً، كقولنا: (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وقياسه من الشكل الأول، وهو (كل "ب" "أ") و(لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فإنه يلزم من انعكاس الكبرى انعكاس النتيجة؛ لأنك إذا جعلتها بعد الانعكاس صغرى للصغرى، عاد إلى الاقتران الثاني من الثاني ناتجاً عكس النتيجة، وهو (لا شيء من "ج" "ب").
The ninth chapter: on verifying what occurs from the conversion of conclusions, premises, entailments, opposites, and other accidents due to the states of the terms. We say: As for what occurs from the conversion of conclusions: if the desired is universal affirmative, as our saying: 'Every B is C,' and it converts as itself to 'Every C is B,' and its syllogism is 'Every B is A' and 'Every A is C,' then the conversion of each of its two premises follows. As for the minor premise, because if you juxtapose the converse of the conclusion with the major premise and say 'Every A is C' and 'Every C is B,' it produces the converse of the minor premise, which is 'Every A is B.' As for the major premise, because if you juxtapose the converse of the conclusion with the minor premise and say 'Every C is B' and 'Every B is A,' it produces the converse of the major premise, which is 'Every C is A.' And if it is universal negative, as our saying: 'No B is C,' and its syllogism is in the first figure: 'Every B is A' and 'No A is C,' then from the conversion of the major premise follows the conversion of the conclusion; because if you make it, after conversion, the minor premise for the minor [premise], it returns to the second conjugation of the second [figure], producing the converse of the conclusion, which is 'No C is B.'
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage from the ninth chapter explains how the conversion of a conclusion follows from the conversion of the premises in categorical syllogisms. The author first considers a universal affirmative desired conclusion, e.g., 'Every B is C,' which converts simply to 'Every C is B.' Given the original syllogism in the first figure with premises 'Every B is A' and 'Every A is C,' the author shows that by taking the converse of the conclusion and combining it with one of the original premises, we obtain the converse of the other premise. Specifically, combining the converse 'Every C is B' with the major premise 'Every A is C' yields the converse of the minor premise, 'Every A is B.' Similarly, combining the converse with the minor premise yields the converse of the major premise. For a universal negative conclusion, e.g., 'No B is C,' the syllogism in the first figure has premises 'Every B is A' and 'No A is C.' The conversion of the major premise 'No A is C' to 'No C is A' allows one to form a new syllogism in the second figure that produces the converse of the original conclusion, 'No C is B.' The author thus demonstrates a method for deriving the converted conclusion through manipulation of premises.

Deriving Converse of Conclusion from Converse of Major Premise (Universal Negative)
Author derives converse of conclusion from converse of major premise for universal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the converse of a universal negative conclusion by converting the major premise in a first-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
For universal negative syllogisms, the converse of the conclusion follows from the conversion of the major premise alone.

The author extends the analysis to universal negative conclusions. By converting the major premise and combining it with the original minor premise in the second figure, we obtain the converse of the original conclusion. This shows symmetry in conversion techniques.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original universal negative syllogism
The author uses: كل ب أ (Every B is A) and لا شيء من أ ج (No A is C) yielding لا شيء من ب ج (No B is C). This is first figure (Celarent).
The terms (ب, أ, ج) are placeholders; substitute with Islamic concepts.
2
Convert the major premise
The major premise لا شيء من أ ج converts simply to لا شيء من ج أ (No C is A).
Universal negative converts simply.
3
Form a new syllogism in the second figure
Take the original minor premise (كل ب أ) as the major premise and the converted major (لا شيء من ج أ) as the minor premise. The new syllogism: كل ب أ, لا شيء من ج أ ⇒ لا شيء من ج ب (No C is B). This is a valid second-figure syllogism (Camestres).
The middle term is 'أ' (A).
4
Verify the result
The original conclusion was لا شيء من ب ج; its converse is لا شيء من ج ب, which matches the derived proposition. Thus, the converse of the conclusion follows from the conversion of the major premise.
This method only requires converting the major premise, not both.

The Syllogism

Correlatives and Opposites in Compound Syllogisms
Proof of opposition transfer via convertibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a logical proof showing that if two pairs of terms are convertible and one pair are opposites, the other pair are also opposites.

Core Idea
Convertibility transfers opposition from one pair to another.

This passage from 'The Eighth Art' demonstrates a powerful syllogistic principle: if two pairs of terms are convertible (co-extensive) and one pair are strict opposites (contradictories), then the other pair are also opposites. The proof uses multiple categorical inferences and conversion properties. Despite missing initial conditions, we can reconstruct the logical chain using the visible steps. This type of reasoning is essential for analyzing relationships between concepts in Islamic sciences, such as attributes in Kalam or oppositions in Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term variables and their relationships
The passage uses Arabic variables أ, ب, ج, د. First, note which pairs are opposites and which are convertible. From the context, أ and ب are opposites (one is true of any subject), and also ج and د are opposites. The passage then introduces convertibility: أ converts with د (كل أ د), and later discusses other convertibilities.
The initial conditions are missing; assume أ opposes ب, ج opposes د, and أ converts with د, and later ب converts with أ under certain conditions.
2
Analyze the first opposition transfer
The author argues: since either ب or أ is true of a subject, and every أ is د, it follows that either ب or د is true. This means ب and د are also opposites. Write this as a syllogism: (ب أو أ) and (كل أ د) → (ب أو د). This is valid because if the subject has أ, it has د; if it has ب, it still satisfies the disjunction with د.
This step requires the disjunctive premise (ب أو أ) which was likely established earlier.
3
Analyze the second opposition transfer
Similarly, because either د or ج is true of the subject, and every د is أ, it follows that either ج or أ is true. Thus ج and أ are opposites. The pattern is identical: opposition transfers through convertibility.
This step mirrors the first, using the opposite pair ج و د and the convertibility د with أ.
4
Establish the convertibility condition
The passage then proves that if أ is predicated of every ب and every ج (and only those), and ب is predicated of every ج, then ب is convertible with أ. This means they have the same extension. The proof uses the fact that whatever أ applies to, ب applies, and vice versa under the given conditions.
This part provides the foundation for the convertibility relations used in earlier steps.
5
Reconstruct the complete argument
Combine the steps: If أ and ب are opposites, and أ and د are convertible, then ب and د are opposites. Similarly, if د and ج are opposites, and د and أ are convertible, then أ and ج are opposites. The final part shows how convertibility can be derived from inclusion relations, completing the logical circle.
The missing initial conditions likely stated the convertibility and opposition pairs outright.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثامن في اعتبار أمور نافعة الاعتبار في صور الحجج وجود "ب" انتفاء "أ" فيلزم انتفاء "د" ومن نفيه وجوده، وكذا بالعكس؛ لأنه إذا صدق على الموضوع إما "ب" وإما "أ" و(كل "أ" "د") صدق إما "ب" وإما "د"، وكما يلزم التقابل بين "ب" و"د"، فكذا بين "ج" و"أ"؛ لأنه إذا صدق على الموضوع إما "د" وإما "ج" و(كل "د" "أ") فهو إما "ج" وإما "أ"، وإما إذا كان "أ" مقولاً على كل "ج" وعلى كل "ب" ولا يقال على غيرهما، وكان "ب" مقولاً على كل "ج" فـ "ب" منعكس في الحمل على كل "أ"؛ إذ هو مقول على كل ما يقال عليه "أ" فكل ما هو "ب" هو "أ" وكل ما هو هو "ب"، وكذا أيضاً إن كان "ج" مقولاً على كل "ب" فهو منعكس أيضاً في الحمل على كل "أ" لما حققناه في قول "ب" على كل "ج".
The Eighth Art: On considering beneficial matters for the forms of arguments. ... the existence of B necessitates the non-existence of A, so the non-existence of D is necessitated, and from its negation its existence [follows], and likewise conversely; because if either B or A is true of the subject, and 'Every A is D', then either B or D is true; and just as the opposition between B and D is necessitated, likewise between C and A; because if either D or C is true of the subject, and 'Every D is A', then it is either C or A. And as for if A is predicated of every C and every B and is not said of other than them, and B is predicated of every C, then B is convertible in predication over every A; for it is predicated of everything of which A is said, so everything that is B is A and everything that is it is B. Likewise also if C is predicated of every B, then it is also convertible in predication over every A according to what we established in the saying of B over every C.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage from 'The Eighth Art' is a proof about the logical relationships between correlative and opposite terms. Although the opening conditions are missing from the OCR, the visible part shows a chain of reasoning: if B and A are opposites (mutually exclusive and exhaustive) and A converts with D, then B and D are also opposites; likewise, if D and C are opposites and D converts with A, then C and A are opposites. The proof uses categorical conversions and the fact that if a term is predicated of everything another is predicated of and more, then conversion holds. Specifically, if A is said of all of B and all of C (and only those), and B is said of all C, then B is convertible with A. This mirrors the logical principle that if the extension of B is contained in A and A is coextensive with B plus C, then B and A are coextensive. In Islamic theology, such reasoning might be used to analyze the relationship between attributes like 'existent' and 'non-existent', but here it remains abstract. The proof is a good exercise in tracking multiple syllogistic inferences, though the missing initial conditions make it challenging to fully parse.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Priority of the Absolute over the Specific
Author argues that absolute syllogism is prior to specific because constitutive, not general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the absolute syllogism is prior to the specific syllogism based on the principle of constitutive priority, and distinguish this from priority by generality.

Core Idea
Priority by constitution is stronger than priority by generality.

This passage addresses a common misconception: that the absolute syllogism is merely more general and thus given priority. The author argues it is far deeper—the absolute is constitutive of the specific. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the structure of logical inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate 'absolute syllogism' (al-qiyās al-muṭlaq) and 'specific syllogism' (al-mukhaṣṣaṣ) in the Arabic. Understand that the absolute is the unrestricted, universal form of syllogism, while the specific is a syllogism with determined matter (e.g., demonstrative or dialectical).
2
Reject the assumption of equality
The author says it is well-known that the absolute does not reflect (i.e., cannot be converted into the specific by a simple logical operation). He dismisses the idea that the two are on equal footing, implying an epistemic hierarchy.
3
Understand the constitutive relation
The priority of the absolute over the specific is not due to generality (عموم), but because the absolute is a constitutive element (muqawwim) of the specific. A constitutive element is something without which the thing cannot be conceived. Knowledge of the specific depends on prior knowledge of the absolute.
The author uses the analogy: just as a composite cannot be known before its parts, the specific cannot be known without the absolute.
4
Apply the principle to matter types
The author extends the argument to dialectical matter versus demonstrative matter. Dialectical matter is more general, but it is not constitutive; it is an accidental attribute. He contrasts this: true matter (demonstrative) when considered as true does not depend on being famous or accepted by the masses.
This shows that priority is reserved for constitutive relations, not just generality.
5
Conclude the epistemic implication
The passage concludes that someone who tries to know a specific syllogism without knowing the absolute has burdened himself excessively. This underscores the necessity of starting with the absolute in any systematic study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمشهور ولا ينعكس فليس ما يجب تقديمه كما يجب تقديم النظر في القياس المطلق على المخصص؛ لتوهم التساوي بين الشيئين، إذ لم يكن تقديم القياس المطلق على المخصص لعمومه، بل لأن نسبته إليه نسبة أمر مقوم ولن يتصور معرفة شيء ما قبل التصور لمقوماته. ولا يخفى أن من أراد تعرّف قیاس خاص دون تعرّف حقيقة القياس مطلقاً فقد كلف نفسه شططاً، والمادة الجدلية وإن كانت أعم من المادة البرهانية فليس نسبتها إليها نسبة عارض غير مقوم، ولهذا إن من نظر إلى المادة الصادقة من حيث هي صادقة فإنه وإن قطع النظر عن شهرتها، وعن كونها غير متبعة عند الجمهور، لم يختل تصديقه، وما أوصل إليه من البرهان.
And it is well-known and does not reflect. So it is not that it must be presented as the consideration of the absolute syllogism before the specific must be presented; because of the assumption of equality between the two things, since the presentation of the absolute syllogism over the specific was not due to its generality, but because its relation to it is that of a constitutive matter, and knowledge of something cannot be conceived before the conception of its constitutive elements. And it is not hidden that whoever wants to know a specific syllogism without knowing the reality of the absolute syllogism has burdened himself with difficulty. And the dialectical matter, even if it is more general than the demonstrative matter, its relation to it is not that of a non-constitutive accident. For this reason, whoever looks at true matter insofar as it is true, even if he disregards its fame and its being not followed by the masses, his assent does not become corrupt, and what he is led to from demonstration.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument about the priority of the absolute syllogism over the specific. The author clarifies that the absolute syllogism is not given priority because it is more general, but because it is a constitutive element (muqawwim) of the specific. Without understanding the absolute, one cannot understand the specific, just as a thing's parts must be known before the whole. The author extends this to dialectical matter vs. demonstrative matter: dialectical matter is more general but non-constitutive, so it does not have the same priority. The point is that true matter, when considered purely as true, does not depend on being famous or accepted by the masses.

A) Because it is more general.

C) Because it is a constitutive element.
D) Because it is more certain.

Key Takeaway
The absolute syllogism is prior to the specific because it is constitutive, not merely more general.

?

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Immediate and Innate Syllogistic Premises
Author divides certain premises from reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish awwaliyyāt from fiṭriyya qiyāsiyya and identify the role of the middle term in the latter.

Core Idea
Certain premises from reason divide into the self-evident and the intuitively syllogistic.

In this passage, the author takes us through the foundational premises that the intellect accepts with certainty. Some require no reasoning beyond understanding the terms; others rely on an innate middle term that the mind intuits. Grasping this distinction is crucial for evaluating the starting points of any syllogistic argument in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition
Look at the proposition in question. What is it claiming? For example, 'The whole is greater than its part.'
2
Check for self-evidence
Ask: Does the intellect assent to this proposition immediately upon conceiving its terms, without any further reasoning? If yes, it is an awwaliyya (primary proposition). Examples include the impossibility of a thing being both A and not-A at the same time.
3
Check for innate syllogism
If the proposition requires a syllogism to be known, ask: Is the middle term of that syllogism known by intuition (badīha)? If yes, it is a fiṭriyya qiyāsiyya. The author gives the example of 'Four is even' — the middle term 'divisible into two equal parts' is intuitively known.
The syllogism is: Every number divisible into two equal parts is even. Four is divisible into two equal parts. Therefore, four is even. The middle term is innate.
4
Practice classification
Take a proposition like 'Two is less than three.' Is it awwaliyya or fiṭriyya? It is awwaliyya because you don't need a syllogism — you see it directly from the meanings of 'two' and 'three'. Contrast with 'Six is even' which requires the same innate syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذ أتينا من ذلك بيان الصور والتأليفات، ومن المواد بما يختص بطرق التصورات فخليق بنا أن نبين مواد غير الحجج التي هي طرق استفادة التصديق، وما ينتهي إليه من المبادئ والمقدمات. فنقول: كل قضية هي مبدأ من مبادئ القياس فإما أن يصدق بها بوجه أو لا يصدق بها، فإن كانت مصدقاً بها، فإما أن يكون ذلك على وجه الضرورة أو الظن فإن كان على وجه الضرورة، فإما أن يكون عن العقل أو قوة خارجة عن العقل، فإن كانت عن العقل فإما أن يكون حصولها له بواسطة أو بغير واسطة، فإن كان بلا واسطة فهي الأوليات التي لا يتوقف العقل في إدراكها على غير تصور مفرداتها، ولا يشعر من استكملت آلة إدراكه، وحصلت عنده المفردات بخلوه عنها في وقت ما كـ التصديق باستحالة اجتماع الضدين وبكون الواحد أقل من الاثنين وليس الحكم بذلك والتصديق به مما يوجب تصوره في نفسه حتى يقال أن ما لا تصور له في النفس فلا سبيل إلى الحكم به، فإن ذلك إنما هو بالنسبة إلى غيره، لا بالقياس إلى نفسه. وما هو بواسطة، فإما أن تكون تلك الواسطة غريزية في العقل أو غير غريزية، فإن كانت غريزية فهي القضايا الفطرية القياس وهي كل قضية أوجب التصديق بها قياساً آخر، حده الأوسط معلوم، حاصل بالبديهة كـ التصديق بزوجية الأربعة لعلمنا بأنها منقسمة بمتساويين وأن كل منقسم بمتساويين فهو زوج. وإن كانت غير غريزية، فإما مكتسبة بالنظر أو غير مكتسبة به، فإن كانت مكتسبة بالنظر فالمتوقف عليها ليس من المبادئ، وإن كانت غير مكتسبة بالنظر فهي المشاهدات، والمجربات والحدسيات، والمتواترات. أما المشاهدات فهي كل قضية صدق العقل بها بواسطة الحس كـ علمنا بحرارة النار، وبرودة الثلج ونحوه. وأما المجربات فهي القضايا التي يصدق العقل بها بواسطة الحس مع التكرار ونوع من النظر كـ العلم بإسهال السقمونيا للصفراء حيث إنه تكرر مراراً، والعقل الصريح يقضي بأنه ليس بالاتفاق وإلا لما دام أو لما كان أكثرياً، وليس لما اختص به کل واحد من الشخصيات، فلم يكن إلا للطبيعة المشتركة أو لملازمها، والحكم به إذ ذاك لا بُدّ على الكلي لما حققناه في الاستقراء. وفي الجملة لا بُدّ في ذلك من اعتبار الحكم على القدر المشترك، لا أعم منه ولا أخص، ومن اعتبار المكان الذي وقعت التجربة فيه والأحوال المقارنة للحكم في كل حالة وإلا كانت التجربة وإن أعطت اليقين في حالة غير معطية لليقين في حالة أخرى، واتجه ما يغالط به بعض المغالطين. وتفارق الاستقراء والتجربة والمشاهدات في أنهما يوجبان الكل
And since we have given from that an explanation of the forms and compositions, and of the materials pertaining to the methods of conceptualizations, it is fitting for us to explain the materials of the other proofs that are methods of acquiring assent, and what ends up as principles and premises. So we say: Every proposition that is a principle of the syllogism is either assented to in some way or not assented to. If it is assented to, then that is either in a necessary way or by opinion. If it is in a necessary way, then it is either from reason or from a power external to reason. If it is from reason, then its acquisition by it is either with an intermediary or without an intermediary. If it is without an intermediary, then they are the primary propositions (awwaliyat) which the intellect does not depend in perceiving them on anything other than the conception of their terms. And one whose faculty of perception is complete does not feel lacking them once the terms are present, at any time, like the assent to the impossibility of the conjunction of opposites and that one is less than two. And the judgment and assent to that are not of such nature as to require its conception in itself such that it is said that what has no conception in the soul has no way to judge it, for that is only relative to something else, not in relation to itself. And what is with an intermediary, then that intermediary is either innate (gharīzī) in the intellect or not innate. If it is innate, then they are the innate syllogistic propositions (al-qadāyā al-fiṭriyya al-qiyāsiyya), which are every proposition whose assent is necessitated by another syllogism whose middle term is known, obtained by intuition, like the assent to the evenness of four because we know that it is divisible into two equal parts and that everything divisible into two equal parts is even. And if it is not innate, then it is either acquired through reflection or not acquired through it. If it is acquired through reflection, then what depends on it is not from the principles. And if it is not acquired through reflection, then they are the observations (mushāhadāt), experiments (mujarrabāt), intuitions (hadsiyyāt), and recurrent reports (mutawātirāt). As for observations, they are every proposition that the intellect affirms through the mediation of sense, like our knowledge of the heat of fire and the cold of snow and the like. As for experiments, they are propositions that the intellect affirms through the mediation of sense along with repetition and a type of reflection, like the knowledge that scammony loosens the bile (yellow bile) because it has been repeated many times, and the clear intellect judges that it is not by coincidence, otherwise it would not be persistent or would not be frequent, and it is not due to what is specific to each individual, so it is only due to the common nature or its concomitant. And the judgment at that time must be universal according to what we have verified in induction. In summary, in that it is necessary to consider the judgment on the common measure, not more general nor more specific, and to consider the place where the experiment took place and the circumstances accompanying the judgment in each case, otherwise the experiment, even if it gives certainty in one case, does not give certainty in another case, and then what some sophists argue would apply. And induction and experiment and observations differ in that they both necessitate the whole.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of the premises that serve as principles of syllogisms. The author begins by noting that after explaining forms and materials for conceptualization, it is fitting to discuss materials for assent. The classification starts by dividing every proposition that is a principle of syllogism into two: those that are assented to (musaddaq bihā) and those that are not. The assented ones are further divided into necessary (ḍarūrī) and opinion (ẓann). Necessary ones come either from reason (ʿaql) or from an external power (e.g., divine inspiration? but not specified further). Reason-based necessary premises are either immediate (without intermediary) or mediated. Immediate ones are the primary propositions (awwaliyat), such as the impossibility of two opposites coexisting or that one is less than two. The author clarifies that these do not require further conception beyond the terms; they are self-evident. Mediated premises are where the intermediary is either innate (gharīzī) in the intellect or not innate. Innate mediated premises are the innate syllogistic propositions (fiṭriyyāt qiyāsiyya), where the middle term is known intuitively, e.g., knowing that four is even because it is divisible into two equal parts and every such number is even. If the intermediary is not innate, then the premise is either acquired through reflection (which would not count as a principle, since it depends on reflection) or not acquired through reflection, which includes observations (mushāhadāt), experiments (mujarrabāt), intuitions (hadsiyyāt), and recurrent reports (mutawātirāt). Observations are sense-based, like knowing fire is hot. Experiments involve repeated observation with a reflective element, like knowing scammony causes bile loosening. The author explains that such knowledge requires identifying the common nature, not individual specifics, and considering conditions like place and circumstance to avoid sophistical objections. The passage ends by noting a distinction between induction (istiqrāʾ), experiment, and observations in that the latter two yield universality ('yūjibān al-kull'). This classification is crucial for understanding the epistemological foundations of syllogistic premises in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
أوليات
فطرية قياسية

Checkpoint
According to the author, which category does the proposition 'Four is even' belong to?
A) Awwaliyya (primary proposition)
B) Fiṭriyya qiyāsiyya (innate syllogistic proposition)
C) Mushāhada (observation)
D) Mujarriba (experiment)

Key Takeaway
Certain premises from reason divide into awwaliyyāt (self-evident without any syllogism) and fiṭriyya qiyāsiyya (which require a syllogism but the middle term is innate).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the proposition 'The same thing cannot be both moving and at rest at the same time in the same respect' as either awwaliyya or fiṭriyya, and explain your reasoning.
?
Construct a fiṭriyya qiyāsiyya using the middle term 'composed of parts' to prove that 'the world is contingent' (if that is your conclusion). Check that the middle term is intuitive.

Empirical Certain Premises: Observations and Experiments
Author divides empirical certain premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a proposition to be a valid experimental premise (mujarriba) and distinguish it from mere observation.

Core Idea
Certain knowledge from sense experience requires repeated observation and rational judgment of common nature.

Beyond the certainties of pure reason, we gain knowledge through the senses and through repeated observation. The author explains how experiments yield universal certainty when properly conducted, and he warns against fallacies that arise from ignoring conditions like place and circumstance. This is essential for evaluating empirical claims in Islamic sciences, especially medicine and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Check if the proposition comes from direct sense
If it is a simple sense perception like 'Fire is hot' or 'Snow is cold', it is a mushāhada (observation). No repetition or reasoning is needed for certainty.
2
Check for repetition and rational judgment
If the proposition involves repeated observation and a judgment that the effect is not accidental but due to a common nature, it is a mujarriba (experiment). Example: scammony loosens bile.
The rational judgment is that the effect is persistent or frequent, and not due to individual characteristics.
3
Verify the conditions for experimental certainty
The author stresses that the judgment must be universal, based on the common nature (not more general or more specific), and must consider the place and circumstances of the experiment. If these conditions are not met, the experiment may not give certainty in other cases.
This avoids the fallacy of generalizing from a single case or ignoring relevant variables.
4
Distinguish from induction
The author notes that induction (istiqrāʾ) differs from experiment and observation in that the latter two necessitate the whole (universal), whereas induction does not necessarily yield universality unless complete.

Premises in Logic: From Fame to Certainty
Author classifies premises into types based on acceptance and source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given premise into one of the thirteen types based on its source and degree of acceptance.

Core Idea
A premise's epistemic strength determines its role.

In this passage, the author systematically classifies the materials (mawadd) that feed into syllogisms. He distinguishes premises by how they are accepted: some are universally famous, some are limited to a group, some come from a single authority, and some are based on conjecture. This classification is essential for understanding which types of arguments are appropriate in different contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of the premise.
Is the premise based on universal agreement, limited group agreement, a single authority, conjecture, or resemblance? This determines its broad category.
2
Determine the mode of acceptance.
Is it assented to without reflection, or does it admit doubt? Famous premises can be doubted upon reflection, while primaries cannot.
The author explicitly contrasts famous premises with innate (fitriyya) ones.
3
Check for resemblance.
Does the premise resemble truth in wording or meaning? If so, it may be an ambiguous proposition (mushabbihāt), e.g., calling wine 'khamr' due to shared inebriation.
4
Evaluate its emotional impact.
Does it affect the soul without being assented to? That would be an imaginative premise (mukhīlāt), used in poetry.
For example, likening honey to feces to discourage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دقائق الحقائق الجزء الثاني المقالة الخامسة: في علم البرهان
الفصل الثاني: في مواد القياس وتعديد أصنافها وما هو مادة كل صنف

طمأنينة النفس سميت أصولاً موضوعة، وإن كان مع النفرة والاستنكار سميت مصادرات. وأما إن كان الثاني، فإما أن يكون ذلك على أن المسلم مشترك فيه، أو على أنه من واحد أو من عدد محصور. فإن كان مشتركاً فيه، فإما أن يكون الاشتراك بين الناس بأجمعهم، أو ما بين طائفة منهم. فما مثل الأول فهي القضايا المشهورة المطلقة وهي ما أوجب التصديق بها اتفاق الكافة عليها كالحكم بحسن الشكر، وقبح الكفران ونحوه. وهي لو خُلّي الإنسان فيها ودواعي نفسه من مبدأ النشوة، من غير التفات إلى الإعراض واكتساب الآداب، وأحكام الشريعة فغير ممتنع أن يتشكك في هذه الأحكام أن لو شكك نفسه، فليست فطرية كما ظن؛ إذ الفطرية لا تشكك فيها، ولا يدخلها احتمال النقيض. وما مثل الثاني فيُسمّى القضايا المشهورة المحدودة كالحكم بقبح ذبح الحيوان وإيلامه. واسم المشهور وإن خُصص بما مثل هذه القضايا، فليس إلا بعرف الاستعمال، وإلا فهو أيضاً يطلق على غيره من القضايا المصدق بها، كانت من الأوليات أو غيرها، لكن ذلك مما لا ينعكس كنفسه. ولا يبعد أن يكون شيء من المشهورات كاذباً؛ بسبب الوقوع في ما يغلط. وما كان منها صادقاً فقد لا يبعد أن يكون تيقنه وتحققه موقوفاً على التبرهن أو أن يكون صدقه موقوفاً على تحقيق شرط خفي، لا يتفطن له، ويظن صدقه مطلقاً كاعتقاد قبح الكذب الذي يستفاد به عصمة دم نبي أو ولي، بإخفائه عن ظالم يقصد قتله، وإنما يكون حقاً إن لو نظر إلى هذا القيد.

وأما إن كان من واحد أو عدد محصور، وكان أو كانوا موثوقاً بقوله أو قولهم فتُسمّى القضايا المقبولة كالأحكام المأخوذة عن الأنبياء والمرسلين، وأهل الحل والعقد من الأئمة المهديين.

وأما إن كان الظن بها لا على سبيل التسليم من الغير كاعتقادنا أن فلاناً يسلم الثغر عند رؤيته، يسار العدو فيُخص بالمظنونات لكن ما كان استناد الظن فيها المشابهة بالحق الصادق، أما في اللفظ كاعتقال كون الدينار باخراً لمشاركته الرطوبة الجليدية في الاسم، أو في المعنى كاعتقال كون النبيذ خمراً لمشاركته له في صفة الإسكار، فيُسمّى المشبهات. وكذا أيضاً ما كان منها مشبهاً بالمشهور، وعند التأمل ليس من المشهور كاعتقال نُصرة الأخ عند كونه ظالماً، أخذاً من قول الجمهور انصر أخاك ظالماً أو مظلوماً وليس المراد به عند التأمل غير كفه عن الظلم.

وأما إن كانت غير مصدق بها، فإما أن تؤثر في النفس تأثيراً يقوم مقام التصديق من وجه أو ليس. فإن كان الأول فهي المخيلات، وهي ما يقال لأجل التغيب في ما يقصد الترغيب فيه والتنفير عما يقصد التنفير عنه كتشبيه العسل بالعذرة؛ لقصد التنفير عنه، وتشبيه الدواء المُستكره بالشراب المُستلذ لقصد الترغيب في شربه. وإن كانت لا تؤثر في النفس تأثيراً يقوم مقام التصديق من وجه، فغير منتفع بها في شيء. فإذن مواد القياس ثلاثة عشر صنفاً، وهي الأوليات، والمشاهدات، والمجربات، والحدسيات، والمتواترات، والمقدمات الفطرية القياس، والوهميات، والمتخيلات. فالأوليات والمشاهدات والمجربات والحدسيات والمتواترات والمقدمات الفطرية القياس والوهميات في المحسوسات هي مواد القياس البرهاني، إلا أن ما حصل لبعض الناس من تجربة أو تواتر، أو حدس، وإن كان حجة عليه مع نفسه، فليس بحجة على غيره؛ لجواز أن لا يحصل لغيره ما حصل له. والمسلمات والمشهورات هي مواد القياس الجدلي. والمقبولات والمظنونات هي مواد القياس الخطابي. والمخيلات هي مواد القياس الشعري. والمشبهات والوهميات في غير المحسوسات هي مواد القياس المغالطي.
Daqaiq al-Haqaiq, Part Two, Article Five: On the Science of Demonstration. Chapter Two: On the materials of the syllogism and enumerating their types and what is the material of each type. The tranquility of the soul were called posited principles, and if with aversion and disapproval they were called postulates. As for the second, it is either that the conceded is shared in, or that it is from one or a limited number. If it is shared in, then either the sharing is among all people, or among a group of them. What is like the first are the absolute famous propositions, which are what unanimous agreement necessitates assent to, like the judgment that gratitude is good and ingratitude is evil and the like. If a person were left to himself and his natural inclinations from the beginning of maturity, without regard to turning away and acquiring etiquette and rulings of the Sharia, it is not impossible to have doubt about these judgments if he doubts himself, so they are not innate as thought; for innate matters do not admit doubt in them, nor does the possibility of the opposite enter them. What is like the second are called the limited famous propositions, like the judgment that slaughtering animals and causing them pain is evil. The name 'famous' even if specified to what is like these propositions, is only by convention of use; otherwise it is also applied to other propositions that are assented to, whether from primaries or others, but that is not convertible by itself. It is not unlikely that something among the famous propositions is false due to falling into error. What among them is true, it is not unlikely that its certainty and verification depend on demonstration, or that its truth depends on the verification of a hidden condition, not noticed, and it is thought to be absolutely true, like the belief in the evil of lying by which the inviolability of the blood of a prophet or saint is preserved, by hiding it from an oppressor intent on killing him; it would only be true if this condition is considered. As for if it is from one or a limited number, and they are trustworthy in their statement, they are called accepted propositions, like rulings taken from prophets and messengers, and those who untie and tie from the guided imams. As for if there is conjecture about them not by way of submission from another, like our belief that so-and-so will secure the pass when he sees the enemy, they are designated as conjectural propositions. But what is based on conjecture of resemblance to the true truth, whether in wording like believing that the dinar is a 'bakh' due to its sharing the name 'icy moisture', or in meaning like believing that wine is khamr due to its sharing the intoxicating quality, is called ambiguous propositions. Likewise also what among them resembles the famous, but upon reflection is not from the famous, like the belief in supporting a brother when he is an oppressor, taking from the saying of the public 'Help your brother whether he is an oppressor or oppressed', but the intended meaning upon reflection is nothing but preventing him from oppression. As for what is not assented to, it either has an effect on the soul that substitutes for assent in some respect or not. If the former, they are imaginative propositions, which are said for the purpose of making absent the thing in what is intended to encourage or discourage, like likening honey to feces to discourage it, and likening the disliked medicine to a pleasant drink to encourage drinking it. If they have no effect on the soul that substitutes for assent in some respect, they are not benefited from in anything. So then the materials of the syllogism are thirteen types: primaries, observations, experiments, intuitions, recurrent reports, innate premises [and] syllogistic [premises], estimative premises, and imaginative premises. The primaries, observations, experiments, intuitions, recurrent reports, innate premises [and] syllogistic [premises], and estimative premises in sensory matters are the materials of the demonstrative syllogism, except that what some people acquire from experiment, recurrent report, or intuition, even if it is a proof for him with himself, is not a proof for others because it is possible that others do not acquire what he acquired. The conceded and famous propositions are the materials of the dialectical syllogism. The accepted and conjectural propositions are the materials of the rhetorical syllogism. The imaginative propositions are the materials of the poetic syllogism. The ambiguous and estimative premises in non-sensory matters are the materials of the sophistical syllogism.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition of the materials (mawādd) of the syllogism (qiyās). The author begins by distinguishing types of premises based on how they are accepted. He first discusses premises that bring tranquility of the soul, called 'posited principles' (uṣūl mawḍūʿa), and those met with aversion, called 'postulates' (muṣādarāt). Then he turns to the case where the premise is conceded by another, either shared or from one person. If shared, it is either universally agreed upon, i.e., 'absolute famous propositions' (al-mashhūra al-muṭlaqa), like the goodness of gratitude, or limited to a group, i.e., 'limited famous propositions' (al-mashhūra al-maḥdūda), like the evil of causing pain to animals. He clarifies that these are not innate (fiṭriyya) because they can admit doubt upon reflection. He also warns that some famous propositions may be false due to error, and even true ones may depend on hidden conditions. Next, he discusses premises from a trustworthy single person or limited group, called 'accepted propositions' (al-maqbūla), like rulings from prophets. Then he covers conjectural propositions (al-maẓnūnāt) which are based on conjecture, and ambiguous ones (al-mushabbihāt) which resemble truth in wording or meaning, giving examples like calling a coin 'bakh' due to a shared name, or calling wine 'khamr' due to shared inebriation. He also mentions propositions that resemble the famous but are not truly so, like misinterpreting the hadith 'Help your brother whether oppressor or oppressed' as supporting oppression instead of preventing it. Finally, he discusses imaginative propositions (al-mukhīlāt) which affect the soul emotionally, used to encourage or discourage, such as likening honey to feces. After this classification, he concludes that the materials of the syllogism are thirteen types, listing them: primaries (awwaliyāt), observations (mushāhadāt), experiments (mujarrabāt), intuitions (ḥadsīyāt), recurrent reports (mutawātirāt), innate premises (al-muqaddamāt al-fiṭriyya) and syllogistic premises (al-qiyāsiyya? — the text joins them as 'al-fiṭriyya al-qiyās'), estimative premises (wahmiyāt), and imaginative premises (mutakhayyilāt). He then assigns these to the five arts: the first group (except wahmiyāt in non-sensory matters) are for demonstration (burhānī); the conceded (musallamāt) and famous (mashhūrāt) for dialectic (jadālī); the accepted (maqbūlāt) and conjectural (maẓnūnāt) for rhetoric (khiṭābī); the imaginative (mukhīlāt) for poetry (shiʿrī); and the ambiguous (mushabbihāt) and estimative in non-sensory matters for sophistry (muġāliṭī).

Checkpoint
According to the author, what distinguishes absolute famous propositions from limited famous propositions?
A) Absolute ones are based on revelation, limited ones on reason.
B) Absolute ones are agreed upon by all people; limited ones by a specific group.
C) Absolute ones are certain; limited ones are conjectural.
D) Absolute ones are about moral judgments; limited ones about physical matters.

Key Takeaway
Premises are classified by their source and mode of acceptance, from universal fame to individual authority to conjecture and imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the statement 'Harming animals is evil' – is this an absolute or limited famous premise? Why?
?
Classify the following: 'The Prophet ﷺ said: "Actions are by intentions"' – is it a primary, accepted, or famous premise? Defend your answer.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Mapping Premises to the Five Arts
Author assigns premise types to the five arts of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to match each of the five logical arts (demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, sophistry) with its appropriate premise types.

Core Idea
Each art has its own materials; using the wrong material yields an invalid argument.

After listing thirteen types of premises, the author assigns them to the five arts of logic. This mapping is crucial: a demonstrative syllogism requires certain premises (primaries, observations, etc.), while a dialectical syllogism uses conceded and famous premises. Using conjectural premises in demonstration would be a mistake. Understanding this assignment helps you choose the right premises for your argumentative context.

Guided Path
1
List the five arts.
The five arts are: demonstration (burhānī), dialectic (jadālī), rhetoric (khiṭābī), poetry (shiʿrī), and sophistry (muġāliṭī).
2
Identify the premise types for demonstration.
Primaries, observations, experiments, intuitions, recurrent reports, innate/syllogistic premises, and estimative premises in sensory matters are for demonstration.
The author adds a caveat: personal experiences are not proof for others.
3
Identify the premise types for dialectic.
Conceded (musallamāt) and famous (mashhūrāt) propositions are for dialectic.
These are based on communal agreement, not certain demonstration.
4
Identify the premise types for rhetoric and poetry.
Rhetoric uses accepted (maqbūlāt) and conjectural (maẓnūnāt) premises. Poetry uses imaginative (mukhīlāt) premises.
Rhetorical arguments aim to persuade, not prove; poetry aims to affect the soul.
5
Identify the premise types for sophistry.
Sophistry uses ambiguous (mushabbihāt) premises and estimative (wahmiyāt) premises in non-sensory matters.
These resemble truth but are false or deceptive.

دقائق الحقائق الجزء الثاني المقالة الخامسة: في علم البرهان
الفصل الثاني: في مواد القياس وتعديد أصنافها وما هو مادة كل صنف

طمأنينة النفس سميت أصولاً موضوعة، وإن كان مع النفرة والاستنكار سميت مصادرات. وأما إن كان الثاني، فإما أن يكون ذلك على أن المسلم مشترك فيه، أو على أنه من واحد أو من عدد محصور. فإن كان مشتركاً فيه، فإما أن يكون الاشتراك بين الناس بأجمعهم، أو ما بين طائفة منهم. فما مثل الأول فهي القضايا المشهورة المطلقة وهي ما أوجب التصديق بها اتفاق الكافة عليها كالحكم بحسن الشكر، وقبح الكفران ونحوه. وهي لو خُلّي الإنسان فيها ودواعي نفسه من مبدأ النشوة، من غير التفات إلى الإعراض واكتساب الآداب، وأحكام الشريعة فغير ممتنع أن يتشكك في هذه الأحكام أن لو شكك نفسه، فليست فطرية كما ظن؛ إذ الفطرية لا تشكك فيها، ولا يدخلها احتمال النقيض. وما مثل الثاني فيُسمّى القضايا المشهورة المحدودة كالحكم بقبح ذبح الحيوان وإيلامه. واسم المشهور وإن خُصص بما مثل هذه القضايا، فليس إلا بعرف الاستعمال، وإلا فهو أيضاً يطلق على غيره من القضايا المصدق بها، كانت من الأوليات أو غيرها، لكن ذلك مما لا ينعكس كنفسه. ولا يبعد أن يكون شيء من المشهورات كاذباً؛ بسبب الوقوع في ما يغلط. وما كان منها صادقاً فقد لا يبعد أن يكون تيقنه وتحققه موقوفاً على التبرهن أو أن يكون صدقه موقوفاً على تحقيق شرط خفي، لا يتفطن له، ويظن صدقه مطلقاً كاعتقاد قبح الكذب الذي يستفاد به عصمة دم نبي أو ولي، بإخفائه عن ظالم يقصد قتله، وإنما يكون حقاً إن لو نظر إلى هذا القيد.

وأما إن كان من واحد أو عدد محصور، وكان أو كانوا موثوقاً بقوله أو قولهم فتُسمّى القضايا المقبولة كالأحكام المأخوذة عن الأنبياء والمرسلين، وأهل الحل والعقد من الأئمة المهديين.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition of the materials (mawādd) of the syllogism (qiyās). The author begins by distinguishing types of premises based on how they are accepted. He first discusses premises that bring tranquility of the soul, called 'posited principles' (uṣūl mawḍūʿa), and those met with aversion, called 'postulates' (muṣādarāt). Then he turns to the case where the premise is conceded by another, either shared or from one person. If shared, it is either universally agreed upon, i.e., 'absolute famous propositions' (al-mashhūra al-muṭlaqa), like the goodness of gratitude, or limited to a group, i.e., 'limited famous propositions' (al-mashhūra al-maḥdūda), like the evil of causing pain to animals. He clarifies that these are not innate (fiṭriyya) because they can admit doubt upon reflection. He also warns that some famous propositions may be false due to error, and even true ones may depend on hidden conditions. Next, he discusses premises from a trustworthy single person or limited group, called 'accepted propositions' (al-maqbūla), like rulings from prophets. Then he covers conjectural propositions (al-maẓnūnāt) which are based on conjecture, and ambiguous ones (al-mushabbihāt) which resemble truth in wording or meaning, giving examples like calling a coin 'bakh' due to a shared name, or calling wine 'khamr' due to shared inebriation. He also mentions propositions that resemble the famous but are not truly so, like misinterpreting the hadith 'Help your brother whether oppressor or oppressed' as supporting oppression instead of preventing it. Finally, he discusses imaginative propositions (al-mukhīlāt) which affect the soul emotionally, used to encourage or discourage, such as likening honey to feces. After this classification, he concludes that the materials of the syllogism are thirteen types, listing them: primaries (awwaliyāt), observations (mushāhadāt), experiments (mujarrabāt), intuitions (ḥadsīyāt), recurrent reports (mutawātirāt), innate premises (al-muqaddamāt al-fiṭriyya) and syllogistic premises (al-qiyāsiyya? — the text joins them as 'al-fiṭriyya al-qiyās'), estimative premises (wahmiyāt), and imaginative premises (mutakhayyilāt). He then assigns these to the five arts: the first group (except wahmiyāt in non-sensory matters) are for demonstration (burhānī); the conceded (musallamāt) and famous (mashhūrāt) for dialectic (jadālī); the accepted (maqbūlāt) and conjectural (maẓnūnāt) for rhetoric (khiṭābī); the imaginative (mukhīlāt) for poetry (shiʿrī); and the ambiguous (mushabbihāt) and estimative in non-sensory matters for sophistry (muġāliṭī).

The five arts each have distinct materials: demonstration uses certain premises, dialectic uses agreed premises, rhetoric uses accepted/conjectural, poetry uses imaginative, and sophistry uses deceptive premises.

The Five Arts of Syllogism and Their Material Premises
Author enumerates thirteen materials of syllogism and assigns each to one of the five arts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five logical arts and match each art with the types of premises that serve as its material.

Core Idea
The epistemic quality of premises assigns a syllogism to its logical art.

In this passage, the author provides a comprehensive classification of syllogistic premises into thirteen types, then distributes them across five arts. Understanding this distribution is essential for recognizing the purpose and strength of any argument in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
List the five arts explicitly named
From the passage, extract the names: برهاني (demonstrative), جدلي (dialectical), خطابي (rhetorical), شعري (poetic), مغالطي (fallacious). Note that each art corresponds to a specific kind of syllogism.
2
Identify the premises for demonstrative syllogism
The passage lists: الأوليات, المشاهدات, المجربات, الحدسيات, المتواترات, المقدمات الفطرية القياس, الوهميات في المحسوسات. These are premises that yield certainty, but note the caveat about تجربة and تواتر being only certain for the individual.
3
Identify the premises for dialectical, rhetorical, poetic, and fallacious arts
Dialectical uses المسلمات and المشهورات; rhetorical uses المقبولات and المظنونات; poetic uses المخيلات; fallacious uses المشبهات and الوهميات في غير المحسوسات.
4
Note the total number of premise types
The author summarizes that there are thirteen types of materials. Try to count them from the lists above. (Hint: some types overlap; the key is that الوهميات is one type split into two subcategories, and المتخيلات/المخيلات might be the same.)
The exact count of thirteen is confirmed by the text: 'فإذن مواد القياس ثلاثة عشر صنفاً'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني: في مواد القياس وتعديد أصنافها وما هو مادة كل صنف
في صفة الإسكار، فيُسمّى المشبهات)، وكذا أيضاً ما كان منها مشبهاً بالمشهور، وعند التأمل ليس من المشهور كـ (اعتقاد نُصرة (الأخ عند كونه ظالماً، أخذاً من قول الجمهور انصر أخاك ظالماً أو مظلوماً وليس المراد به عند التأمل غير كفه عن الظلم.
وأما إن كانت غير مصدق بها، فإما أن تؤثر في النفس تأثيراً يقوم مقام التصديق من وجه أو ليس فإن كان الأول فهي المخيلات، وهي ما يقال لأجل التغيب في ما يقصد الترغيب فيه والتنفير عما يقصد التنفير عنه كـ تشبيه العسل بالعذرة؛ لقصد التنفير عنه، وتشبيه الدواء المُستكره بالشراب المُستلذ لقصد الترغيب في شربه وإن كانت لا تؤثر في النفس تأثيراً يقوم مقام التصديق من وجه، فغير منتفع بها في شيء، فإذن مواد القياس ثلاثة عشر صنفاً، وهي الأوليات، والمشاهدات ،والمجربات والحدسيات والمتواترات والمقدمات الفطرية القياس والوهميات والمتخيلات ،فالأوليات والمشاهدات ،والمجربات والحدسيات، والمتواترات والمقدمات الفطرية القياس والوهميات في المحسوسات هي مواد القياس البرهاني، إلا أن ما حصل لبعض الناس من تجربة أو تواتر، أو حدس، وإن كان حجة عليه مع نفسه، فليس بحجة على غيره؛ لجواز أن لا يحصل لغيره ما حصل له، والمسلمات والمشهورات، هي مواد القياس الجدلي، والمقبولات، والمظنونات هي مواد القياس الخطابي، والمخيلات هي مواد القياس الشعري والمشبهات والوهميات في غير المحسوسات هي مواد القياس المغالطي.
Chapter Two: On the materials of the syllogism, enumeration of its types, and what is the material of each type. Regarding the property of intoxication, it is called 'equivocal things' (mushtabihat). Likewise, also what was similar to the well-known (mashhur) but upon reflection is not from the well-known, like holding the opinion of supporting one's brother when he is an oppressor, taken from the common saying 'Support your brother whether oppressor or oppressed'—though its intended meaning upon reflection is nothing other than preventing him from injustice. As for those [premises] that are not assented to [with certainty], they either affect the soul in a way that takes the place of assent to some extent, or not. If the former, they are 'imaginary things' (mukhayyilat), and they are what is said for the sake of influencing what is intended to be desired or repelled from what is intended to be avoided, like comparing honey to excrement to repel from it, or comparing unpleasant medicine to pleasant drink to encourage drinking it. If they do not affect the soul in a way that takes the place of assent to some extent, then they are of no benefit at all. Therefore, the materials of the syllogism are thirteen types: primary intuitions (awwaliyat), observed things (mushahadat), experiential things (mujarrabat), intuitive things (hadsivat), recurrent things (mutawatirat), innate premises (muqaddimat fitriyya), syllogistic [premises] (qiyasiyya), estimative things (wahmiyat), and imaginative things (mutakhayyilat). The primary intuitions, observed things, experiential things, intuitive things, recurrent things, innate premises, syllogistic [premises], and estimative things regarding sensibles are the materials of demonstrative syllogism. However, what a person obtains through experience, recurrence, or intuition, though it is evidence for himself, is not evidence for others, because it is possible that others have not obtained what he obtained. The accepted things (musallamat) and well-known things (mashhurat) are the materials of dialectical syllogism. The accepted traditions (maqbulat) and conjectural things (maznunat) are the materials of rhetorical syllogism. The imaginary things (mukhayyilat) are the materials of poetic syllogism. The equivocal things (mushtabihat) and estimative things regarding non-sensibles are the materials of fallacious syllogism.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the materials (propositions) used in syllogisms according to the five arts of logic. It begins by discussing the category of 'equivocal premises' (mushtabihat), which are similar to the well-known but upon scrutiny are not truly well-known, giving the example of the saying 'Support your brother whether oppressor or oppressed'—a common claim that upon reflection is meant to prevent oppression, not endorse it. The author then distinguishes between premises that are assented to (muṣaddaq bihā) and those that are not. Among the non-assented, if they affect the soul like assent, they are 'imaginary things' (mukhayyilat), used in poetry to encourage or discourage by analogy, as in comparing honey to excrement to repulse or medicine to pleasant drink to attract. If they have no such effect, they are useless. Next, the author lists the thirteen types of syllogistic materials: primary intuitions (self-evident truths), observed things (empirical), experiential (inductive), intuitive (hads), recurrent (massively transmitted), innate premises (fitri), syllogistic (conclusions from previous syllogisms), estimative (about sensibles), and imaginative (mutakhayyilat). These are then assigned to the five arts: (1) Demonstrative syllogism uses the first seven and estimative things about sensibles, but the author notes that experiential, recurrent, and intuitive premises are only certain for the one who has experienced them, not for others. (2) Dialectical syllogism uses accepted (musallamat) and well-known (mashhurat) premises. (3) Rhetorical syllogism uses accepted traditions (maqbulat) and conjectural (maznunat) premises. (4) Poetic syllogism uses imaginative things (mukhayyilat). (5) Fallacious syllogism uses equivocal things (mushtabihat) and estimative things about non-sensibles. The passage thus shows how the epistemic quality of premises determines the logical art.

Key Terms

القياس البرهاني
المخيلات
المشبهات

Key Takeaway
The logical art of a syllogism is determined solely by the epistemic nature of its premises.

Burhan Inni: Demonstration of 'That' and Its Two Subtypes
Author defines burhan inni and its two subtypes with conditions for certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish absolute burhan inni from daleel and identify the conditions for certainty.

Core Idea
Burhan inni proves existence without revealing the cause; its subtypes differ by the relationship of the middle term.

This lesson clarifies the second main type of demonstration, burhan inni, which gives knowledge that something is the case without explaining why. The author distinguishes two subtypes: one where the middle term is a mere concomitant, and another where it is an effect. Understanding this distinction helps in evaluating the certainty of arguments from signs and effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of burhan inni
Read the first sentence: it yields causality only in assent, meaning knowledge that something is the case without the reason why. Contrast this with burhan limmi (which gives the cause).
Burhan inni answers 'that it is', not 'why it is'.
2
Distinguish the two subtypes
The author divides burhan inni into two: absolute inni (middle term is neither cause nor effect but concomitant) and daleel (middle term is an effect of the major). Note the examples: chills and hot urine for absolute inni; effect-to-cause for daleel.
Absolute inni uses concomitants (lazim); daleel uses effects.
3
Examine the condition of perfect causality
For certainty, the causal link must be perfect: if the middle term is a cause, it must be complete; if it is an effect, the cause must be single. The text gives counterexamples of imperfect causality: sensitivity is only a partial cause; heat has multiple possible causes.
Imperfect causality leads to uncertainty (zann), not certainty (yaqin).
4
Understand the limitation of perpetual certainty
Even perfect burhan does not give perpetual certainty. Burhan limmi may fail if the cause is not always operative; burhan inni does not guarantee perpetual certainty because the causal relation may not hold at all times.
This is an important epistemological nuance: certainty is about the argument's validity, not its eternal applicability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان مفيداً للمية بالتصديق فقط، فيسمى برهان الأن، هو إما أن يكون الحد الأوسط فيه لا علة للأكبر في الأصغر، ولا معلولاً له، بل مقارناً ومساوياً له، وسواء كانا معلولين لعلة واحدة أو ليس، وإما أن يكون تابعاً للأكبر ومعلولاً له، فما مثل الأول يسمى برهان الأن مطلقاً، وهو كالاستدلال بقشعريرة البدن ونارية البول على الحمى الغب، وليس الحد الأوسط علّة للأكبر في الأصغر، ولا معلولاً له، وإنما هما لازمان للصغرى، وما مثل الثاني فيُخصّ باسم الدليل، فإنه كما يلزم وجود المعلول من علته يلزم وجود العلة من معلولها، وكل ذلك فمفيد لليقين لكن شرط أن يكون الحد الأوسط عند كونه لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر على كمال سببيته الموجبة لوجود الأكبر في الأصغر، وعند كونه معلولاً كما تكون العلة له متحدة، كما في الأمثلة المذكورة. وأما إن كان السبب المعطي للوجود ليس على السببية الكاملة، إما لأنه غير بين أو لأنه ليس سبباً وحده بالذات، بل جزء السبب، كما في الاستدلال بالحساس على وجود الحيوان في زيد، فإنه وإن كان علة بوجه ما للحيوان، فليس هو المكاملة لوجود الحيوان في زيد؛ إذ ليس الحساس مرادفاً للحيوان حتى يكون مفهومه مفهومه، ثم لو كان لما كان القياس مفيداً بل الحساس هو شيء ما ذو حس فقط، ومفهومه فأكمل من مفهومه، فإنه معها أنه شيء ما ذو حس جسم متحرك نام مغتذ، وليس يلزم من وصفك لشيء ما أنه ذو حس أن يكون جسماً ومتحركاً ومتغذياً ونامياً، إلا بالنظر إلى مطابقة الوجود له وبالاستقراء، وذلك مما يوجب جعله يقينياً، وأما بالنظر إلى العقل فإنك لو فرضت فيه شيئاً ذا حس ونفس من غير أن يكون له شيء من باقي الصفات المذكورة لما امتنع عليك تصوره. وأما إن كانت العلل لما جعل حداً أوسط متعددة، كما في السخونة فإنها قد تكون عن الشمس، وقد تكون عن المرض، وقد تكون عن الحركة وعن ذلك، فليس يلزم الاستدلال بوجود السخونة على إحدى هذه العلل؛ لجواز أن تكون العلة لها غيرها. وليس كل واحد من برهان اللم والأن، وإن أفاد اليقين، فما يجب إعطائه لليقين دائماً، أما برهان اللم فلجواز أن يكون السبب فيه لم يعط السببية دائماً، بل في وقت إما معيناً أو غير معيّن، وأما برهان الأن فغير معط لدوام اليقين، وإن كانت النسبة في نفس الأمر يجب أن تكون دائمية لما أعطاه من السبب؛ إذ ما هو النسبة
And if it yields causality only in assent, it is called demonstration of 'that' (burhan inni). It is either such that the middle term is not a cause of the major in the minor, nor an effect of it, but rather concomitant and coextensive with it, whether both are effects of one cause or not; or it is such that the middle term is a follower and effect of the major. What is like the first is called absolute demonstration of 'that', like inferring from goosebumps and the heat of urine to tertian fever; the middle term is not a cause of the major in the minor, nor an effect of it, rather both are concomitants of the minor. What is like the second is specifically called the indicator (dalil), for just as the existence of the effect follows from its cause, so the existence of the cause follows from its effect. All that yields certainty, but on condition that the middle term, when it is for the existence of the major in the minor, is at the perfection of its causality necessitating the existence of the major in the minor; and when it is an effect, the cause is unified, as in the examples mentioned. And if the cause giving existence is not at perfect causality, either because it is not evident or because it is not a cause by itself alone but rather part of the cause, as in inferring from sensitivity to the existence of animal in Zayd, for although it is in some respect a cause of animal, it is not the complete cause for the existence of animal in Zayd, since sensitivity is not synonymous with animal such that its concept is its concept; if it were, the syllogism would not be useful. Rather, sensitivity is something that has sensation only, and its concept is more complete than its concept, for it includes that it is something having sensation, a body, moving, nourished, growing; and it does not follow from your description of something that it has sensation that it is a body, moving, nourished, and growing, except by considering its correspondence to existence and by induction, and that necessitates making it certain. But from the perspective of the mind, if you suppose in it something having sensation and soul without having any of the remaining attributes, it would not be impossible to conceive. And if the causes for what is taken as the middle term are multiple, as in heat, for it may be from the sun, or from illness, or from motion, and so on, then inferring from the existence of heat to one of these causes does not follow, because it is possible that the cause is something else. And not every one of burhan limmi and burhan inni, even if it yields certainty, gives certainty perpetually. As for burhan limmi, because the cause may not give causality always, but only at a particular time, either definite or indefinite. As for burhan inni, it does not give perpetual certainty, even if the relation in reality must be perpetual for what it gives of the cause; because what is the relation...
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of burhan by detailing the second main type: burhan inni (demonstration of 'that'). The author subdivides burhan inni into two categories. The first, called absolute burhan inni, has a middle term that is neither the cause nor the effect of the major but is concomitant (mutlaq) and coextensive with it; both may be effects of a common cause or not. The example given is inferring a specific fever from symptoms like chills and hot urine—here the symptoms are not the cause of the fever but are concomitants of the disease. The second subtype is called daleel (indicator), where the middle term is an effect of the major; this is reasoning from effect to cause, like inferring the cause from its effect. However, for such reasoning to yield certainty, there must be a perfect causal relationship: when the middle term is a cause, it must be the complete cause; when it is an effect, the cause must be single and uniform. The author then discusses cases where the causal connection is imperfect: either the middle term is not the complete cause but only a partial cause (like sensitivity for animality—sensitivity is a necessary concomitant but not sufficient for animality because animality includes other attributes like growth and motion), or the cause is multiple (like heat having many possible sources, so inferring a specific cause from heat is not certain). Finally, the author notes that even perfect burhan does not give perpetual certainty in all circumstances: burhan limmi may fail if the cause is not always operative, and burhan inni does not guarantee perpetual certainty because the connection may be accidental in time.

Key Terms
برهان الأن
الدليل
لازمان
السببية الكاملة

Key Takeaway
Burhan inni provides knowledge of 'that' but not 'why', and its validity depends on the perfection of the causal relationship.
